Enhancing Circular Economy 
readiness through best 
practices and tools
The case of the MATChE Project         
Master thesis project
Design & Innovation
June 2018 
Jeppe Ragner Sloth-Olsen
Nanna la Cour Sonne
==PAGE:2==
==PAGE:3==
page I
 Abstract
The aim of this thesis is to identify and systematize tools for Circular Economy (CE), within the 
scope of MATChE, to integrate tool suggestions as part of the MATChE readiness assessment. 
The MATChE platform is an initiative, managed by DTU, that aims to support manufacturing 
companies on their journey towards CE and increase circular businesses in Denmark.  
CE has received significant attention as an approach to reconcile ecological systems and economic 
growth and stand in contrast to the traditional linear ‘take-make-dispose’ economy. 
The thesis starts with an identification of tools for CE that are applicable in the manufacturing 
industry. Through a Systematic Literature Review of 453 articles, 49 tools were identified and 
extracted. These tools were classified on the basis of 11 classification criteria, among others lin-
king the tools’ relevance to 30 CE dimensions, resulting in a searchable Backstage Framework. 
Through the synthesis of creative and systematic methods for understanding the user context, the 
backstage framework was embodied into a prototype of the final support concept: Toolr. 
Toolr is a website that provides an interface to the backstage framework. Toolr is integrated as 
part of the MATChE readiness assessment through direct tool suggestions. These tools match 
the company’s readiness profile and can be explored in detail on the Toolr website. Here it is also 
possible to take a more explorative approach using the different filters to seek tools from the 
backstage framework. Toolr furthermore consists of information pages of each tools that func-
tion as a translation of the original article. This aims to enhance the companies’ ability to identify 
relevance and comprehend the core value within the tool. 
Based on this outcome, the authors expect that the MATChE team can benefit from the insights 
and knowledge produced by this thesis and include it in the further development of the MATChe 
platform.

 Learning objectives
Analysis
 -
Define relevant scientific knowledge within the field of Circular Economy in relation to linear 
economy. 
 -
Clarify the concept of MATChE and the surrounding context in which our support solution 
should be integrated and functioning.
 -
Demonstrate methodological skills within systematic literature review.
 Design & Evaluation
 -
Categorise tools found in the Systematic Literature Review (SLR) to enable and enhance the 
user’s identification of relevant tools. 
 -
Investigate needs from users and stakeholders and translate these into formalized require-
ments for further development of solutions. 
 -
Synthesize the solution space by combining systematic and creative methods to explore and 
generate ideas. 
 -
Develop a prototype to be used as a boundary object for test and evaluation. 
 -
Evaluate the usefulness, usability and potential application of the support solution.
 Overall project
 -
To demonstrate project management skills, by planning, managing and executing all phases 
of the master thesis project using a main guiding methodology.
 -
To summarise the advantages and disadvantages on the process carried out in the project and 
reflect on that as well as learnings from the process. 
==PAGE:7==
page V
 Motivation 
The relationship between industry and environment is vital for industrial business performance. 
Environmental impacts have increased pressure on industrial businesses, and at the micro level, 
many companies have observed how the linear economic system increases their exposure to risks, 
especially concerning volatility in resource prices and vulnerability to new legislations. In spite of 
the increased popularity of the concept, and that circular production systems are at the intersec-
tion of different research areas, little is known about the actual implementation across industrial 
sectors. Companies seem to struggle to identify the value proposition to their business, as well as 
actually being able to implement circular initiatives. 
Research on circular economy and how to transition is an emerging field and the amount of 
research produced is rapidly increasing. But this knowledge rarely seems to go further than the 
academic spheres. 
Thus, the motivation behind this master thesis is to make this knowledge explicit and directly 
relevant for another network: the manufacturing industry. This thesis aims to do that, by:
1. Systematically reviewing tools for Circular Economy in the scope of MATChE.
2. Developing and conceptualizing a support tool that enables manufacturing companies’ iden-
tification of relevant tools based on their MATChE readiness assessment.
3. Uncovering potential research gaps that could support the industry’s future transition.

 Planning
The thesis has been structured into different phases divided by pre-defined milestones; “Identifi-
cation of tools for Circular Economy”, “Systematisation of tools for Circular Economy”, “Sup-
port tool development” and “Evaluation and test of the support tool”. Additionally, a stage zero 
has been added – giving time to familiarize ourselves with the research area, forming a theoretical 
basis, investigating literature of the field and planning the project and the Systematic Literature 
Review. 
Appendix 1 contains further information about the planning of the project including Gantt charts.

==PAGE:12==
page X
 Thesis structure 
This thesis consists of 8 Chapters. Figure 2 visualises the structure and content of the thesis. 
Chapter 7 & 8 contains references and appendices.  
Chapter 1 starts with a literature review of the area of research and describes the background for 
the project. The chapter ends with an identification of the focus for the thesis.
Chapter 2 highlights the methodology used within the thesis and presents the scope and delimi-
tation of the project. The chapter ends with the establishment of success criteria to be used in 
the evaluation of the project. 
Chapter 3 presents the development of the backstage framework as part of the Descriptive Stu-
dy 1. It compiles the first two phases of the thesis: Identification of tools for circular economy 
and the systematisation of tools. The chapter ends with the establishment of a goal for the fol-
lowing stage, Prescriptive Study. 
Chapter 4 presents the development and embodiment of the Support tool carried out in the 
Prescriptive Study, by integrating the backstage framework into a holistic concept. The chapter 
ends with a presentation of a prototype of the support tool to be used as a boundary object for 
tests and evaluation. 
Chapter 5 presents the developed evaluation plan and result of this, as part of Phase 4 with focus 
on whether the support helped to achieve the aim of the thesis in relation to articulated success 
criteria. 
Lastly, this leads to Chapter 6, which discusses the main contributions and implications of the 
process and ends with a conclusion as well as limitations, reflections and recommendations for 
further work.

page XII
 Reading Guide 
This thesis report is written for other engineers, designers, researchers or anyone else that could 
have interest in this subject and project experience.
The thesis is meant to be read coherently from beginning (Chapter 1) to end (Chapter 7), yet 
related appendices (Chapter 9) accompany the thesis, offering the reader the possibility of going 
into more detail about the work presented in the report. These are referred to with ‘Appendix’ and 
a number according to the order in which they appear in the thesis, e.g. (Appendix 1) is the first 
appendix presented. Furthermore, Chapter 8 includes list of References. 
 Abbreviations

==PAGE:15==
page XIII
Nomenclature 
Article, Paper, Records, Studies, Material
In the thesis the terms article, paper, records and studies will be used interchangeably to describe 
published literature.
Backstage framework
Backstage framework refers to the spreadsheet or database containing filterable information on 
tools and articles, used to deliver the content to the support tool. In more functional terms, the 
backstage framework can be referred to as database. 
Company
The company term defines a legal entity formed by a group of individuals to operate a business 
enterprise. The company is sometimes referred to as being ‘the user’ of the support. Referring to 
the company, it is generally understood how the support can help the transition. See also nomen-
clature for user. 
Grey literature
A term defining material and research that is not published in a traditional academic or commer-
cial context. This could be documents, report, papers and projects from government's, interest 
groups, NGO’s, private or public companies and consultancies.
Tool
Tool(s) is used as a general term throughout the report covering: tools, methods, guides, pro-
cedures, techniques, frameworks and other types of solutions that based on an input can give a 
prescriptive output. Tools can be used in a wide range of areas and ways (see support tool). 
Network
Network is used to describe a system of actors/companies/sectors that on many levels are inter-
linked by common practices and fields of work. Network is in this thesis mainly used to distingu-
ish the academic network and network of manufacturing industries. 
Support tool
In the report the term support tool refers to a solution that by any means have a positively influ-
ence and helps a person with a certain task in a given context. This could be by providing the user 
with methodologies, strategies, procedure, methods, techniques, information sources, guidelines, 
etc. The support can be delivered in a wide range of forms and formats. 
Transition 
The term transition is used to describe an event that entails a change, and in this thesis, a shift 
from current regimes into sustainable ones. 
User
The term user refers to a singular individual, most often as part of a company where they fill out 
a role. The term user is used, as opposed to company, when more direct actions are described that 
requires a person.

Chapter 1
This chapter introduces the relevant literature connected to this research 
project and thus the research field and context which this thesis seeks 
to contribute to. The areas and disciplines will continue to expand as 
the project progresses and the understanding of the subject increases. 
The aim of this chapter is to draw the context in which this research has 
taken place and evolved from.
The authors firstly give an overall introduction to the emergence of the 
concept of CE. Both internationally and specifically in the Danish con-
text. Secondly the authors introduce the MATChE platform. Finally, 
the authors will draw an initial conclusion about where it is possible to 
create a meaningful impact.

1.1 Setting the Scene 
1.1.1 The need for a new economic model for sustainable growth 
Anthropogenic pressures on the Earth System has reached such a level that we, human beings, are 
the dominant drivers of global environmental changes (Rockström et al, 2009). We are living in 
the Anthropocene epoch. In an era where the world's population is growing rapidly, expected to 
reach 9.7 billion by 2050 (United Nations 2017), the need for managing our finite natural resour-
ces in a sustainable manner is becoming critical.
The main challenge of sustainable development is the unbalance between human lifestyle and 
nature. This can be seen depicted in Figure 3, which illustrates how the current growing econo-
mic system with a linear flow of materials and energy collides with the physically shrinking parent 
system: nature (Korhonen et al. 2018; Rockström et al. 2009). 
Economy: A physically 
growing subsystem
Lead to resource overuse, wastes 
and emissions release 
Linear 
ﬂows of 
materials
and energy
Unsustainability 
head collision 
Nature: A shrinking parent system
Figure 3 - Nature: A shrinking parent system (Reproduction of ﬁgure from Rockström et al. 2009)
There is a need to change our ‘take, make, dispose’ and linear way of living and instead, live within 
an economy that does not generate excessive waste and whereby waste is utilized as a resource: a 
circular economy model for optimal resource efficiency.
==PAGE:21==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II

page 3
1.1.2 Circular Economy 
The concept of circularity, especially in the way we produce and consume, is not a novelty and 
cannot be traced back to one single date or author but has been a school of thought emerging 
now and then throughout history. Therefore, the idea of a circular economy is built on prede-
cessors such as Environmental Economics (Pearce & Turner 1990), Natural Capitalism (Hawken 
et al. 1999), Regenerative Design (Lyle 1994), Spaceman' economy (Boulding 1966), Industrial 
Ecology (Erkman 1997), Performance Economy (Stahel 2010), Cradle-to-cradle (McDonough & 
Braungart 2002), Biomimicry (Benyus 1997) and Blue Economy (Pauli 2010). 
Circular Economy has really gained momentum since the establishment of the Ellen MacArthur 
Foundation (EMF) in 2010 and their definition from 2012 (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 2012, 
p.7) is recognized as the most prominent and employed definition (Kirchherr et al. 2017; Geiss-
doerfer et al. 2017; Schut et al. 2015):
“[CE] an industrial system that is restorative or regenerative by intention and design. It replaces the 
‘end-of-life’ concept with restoration, shifts towards the use of  renewable energy, eliminates the use of  
toxic chemicals, which impair reuse, and aims for the elimination of  waste through the superior design 
of  materials, products, systems, and, within this, business models.”
The Ellen MacArthur Foundation (2015a, p.14) also states CE as an economy that:
 “would provide multiple value creation mechanisms decoupled from the consumption of  finite resour-
ces. The concept rests on three principles: preserve and enhance natural capital, optimise yields from 
resources in use, and foster system effectiveness”. 
The linear economy relies on resources and energy in an amount that is increasingly unfit for the 
system it operates in and seeking efficiency alone will only delay the unavoidable situation where 
finite resources are used up. The motivation behind CE is therefore to change the entire economic 
system (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 2012, p.22). The idea behind this system thinking is illustra-
ted with the Circular Economy System Diagram in Figure 4 which outlines the fundamentals for 
CE (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 2015a, p.24)
The system diagram is centred around a life cycle as we know it; from raw material to consumer 
goods, but with two integrated feedback loops: a biological cycle and a technical cycle. These cy-
cles provide the crucial system change, where one system’s waste provides the next system’s input. 
With the report Towards the Circular Economy (2012), EMF and McKinsey & Company de-
monstrated how Circular Economy is beneficial to society in addition to environmental aspects. 
It concretises the incentive that many companies lack in order to think sustainably. Namely the 
economic perspective. By circulating just 23% of all materials in the EU alone, it is estimated that 
approximately USD 630 billion can be saved annually (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 2012, p.80).

 Figure 4 - Ellen MacArthur CE system diagram (Ellen Macarthur 2015a)
1.1.3 Circular Economy deﬁnition  
Circular Economy is a broad term and critics claim that it lacks discursive and practical consensus. 
Blomsma and Brennan (2017) describe CE as a ‘umbrella concept’ that offers a new framing of 
various prolonging resource strategies. The term umbrella concept was originally introduced in 
1999 by Hirsch and Levin who defined it as “a broad concept or idea used loosely to encompass 
and account for a set of diverse phenomena” (1999). One thing the Ellen MacArthur definition 
has been specifically criticized for by Murray et al. (2017) and Kirchherr et al.(2017) is the lack of 
mention of sustainability and namely the missing social dimension. In this work the term CE is 
understood as an abstract concept, a philosophy, that embraces or works together with preceding 
schools of thought towards a sustainable world. Following ‘Our Common Future’ (Brundtland 
1987), also known as the Brundtland Report, Sustainable development is defined as:
“(…) development that meets the needs of  the present without compromising the ability of  future 
generations to meet their own needs.” 
The parallel research project to MATChE, CIRCit (CIRCit Norden 2018), has taken the Ellen 
MacArthur definition and amended the phrase “with a strong sustainability foundation”, so the 
definition in its full length reads:

page 5
“CE defined as an economy that provides multiple value-creation mechanisms which are decoupled 
from the consumption of  finite resources, with a strong sustainability foundation.”
This will be the definition of CE throughout this project.
1.1.4 Transition to a Circular Economy
As stated in the introduction, the relationship between industry and environment is not only 
crucial for the future of the latter, but also for industrial business performance. Environmental 
changes have meant an increased pressure on industrial businesses, most notably through volatili-
ty in resource prices and vulnerability to supply restrictions which means that the transition to the 
CE is becoming more and more inevitable (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 2013; Lieder & Rashid 
2016b; Franco 2017). Such a transition will require insights to transition practices within socio-te-
chnical systems (Jackson et al. 2014; Gorissen et al. 2016).
Transition scholars have developed several frameworks and theories with the aim to increase our 
understanding of how changes in transitions and systems occur over a period of time (Jackson et 
al. 2014; Gorissen et al. 2016).  The focus of this field of literature lies particularly in the insights it 
feeds into how dynamic complex systems, and innovation processes, are in reality because of the 
diversity of elements in social-technical systems. Actors (individual stakeholder or organisations) 
all operate within institutions (social and technical norms, regulations, legislation and standard 
practices), both shaped by existing knowledge and available technologies (Jackson et al. 2014; 
apud Markard et al. 2012). It is hard, if not impossible, to illustrate the network around Circular 
Economy as a concept. The closest we are able to illustrate a network is to understand that the 
network deals with both active and passive elements, including actors, media and artefacts. Active 
in the sense of dealing with Circular Economy, passive in the sense of not doing so. Both types of 
elements have a bearing on the development of CE, in the future, present and the past. However, 
there are many concepts in the field of networking theory that can increase the understanding of 
the network and the transition to CE.
Linear economy dominates our society and serves as a model for an infinite array of practices, 
proficiencies, actors etc., and therefore, the notion of a circular economy also addresses an equally 
large market as well as activities. As stated by Wells (2013) incremental changes within establis-
hed systems will not challenge the core of the business models that is the foundation for much 
unsustainable activity. And the purpose of Socio-Technical transitions are long-term in order to 
change the dominant culture and practices within these unsustainable systems (Gorissen et al. 
2016; Jurgilevich et al. 2016). 
A circular economy transition is therefore something that will oblige a system’s view with initia-
tives on both macro- (e.i., city, province, region, nation), meso- (e.i. eco-industrial parks or other 
types of interfirm clusters) and micro-level (i.e. single firms) (Ghisellini et al. 2016; Murray et al. 
2017; Franco 2017). 
==PAGE:24==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 6
As of today, much of the research within the field of transition to CE has been done at Macro-le-
vel and only little of the implementation at Micro-level (Lieder & Rashid 2016b; Franco 2017). 
Based on a literature review, Lieder and Rashid (2016) suggest a comprehensive framework for 
CE implementation that targets the system’s view (see Figure 5). The framework is based on two 
concurrent strategies: Top-down which operates through public institutions and a (inter-)national 
effort (e.g. legislation and policy, support infrastructure and social awareness) and Bottom-up, 
which operates through industry and an effort made by individual companies (e.g. Collaborati-
ve business models, Product design, Supply chain, Information and communication technology 
(ICT)) (Lieder & Rashid 2016a, p.47). 
 Figure 5 - Proposed CE implementation strategy applying top-down  
and bottom-up approach (ﬁgure based on Lieder & Rashid 2016a)
Lieder and Rashid (2016a) found that the important point is that initiatives must be done simul-
taneously if a transition is to succeed. This transition is based on known technologies, but also 
through the development of new technologies and through a new way of thinking of business 
models (Besenbacher 2017). The development of the former, Technological, plays several roles in 
relation to the environment. With the industrial revolution came a new consumer society that was 
and is not sustainable. Furthermore, technology is a largely variable size, and this can lead to new 
and unknown issues, also known as rebound effects, in terms of new expectations for everyday 
life that increase consumption. Conversely, new technologies can solve environmental problems 
and be a way to reduce the environmental impact of products (Jørgensen 2009, p.124). Therefore, 
transition work needs to incorporate acknowledgement of the unknown; hereby the existence 
of uncertainty, and processes that allows exploration, experiments and reflexivity (Jackson et al. 
2014).

1.1.5 Circular Economy in the Danish industry 
Denmark has many great examples of companies working with CE, from smaller CE start-ups 
like Vigga offering quality baby clothes on a subscription model (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 
2015b, p.26), or the online-based Refurb which buys used IT equipment, refurbishes it and resells 
it with a new life (DI 2018). All the way to the large scale with Kalundborg Industrial Symbiosis 
as a class example on buying and selling waste resources to closing loops in industry production 
(EMF 2018a) or Maersk Line implementing cradle-to-cradle material passports for its new line of 
Triple-E containerships (EMF 2018b).
Although many examples can be found, CE is still a fairly unknown concept in Denmark. In a 
survey among 610 Danish companies, only 29% of companies knew of CE (58% for larger com-
panies with 51-250 employees) (Miljøstyrelsen 2017). Asked directly about initiatives that are part 
of CE, 56% already worked with reducing waste in production, 35% used recycled materials and 
24% worked with deferent types of product life extension design initiatives (Miljøstyrelsen 2017).
The survey also shows that 88% of the companies showed a positive attitude towards CE af-
ter being introduced to the concept with the main reasons being ‘Positive environmental effect’ 
(87%) and thereafter ‘Resource scarcity’ (25%), ‘Economic potential’ (24%) and ‘Market growth’ 
(23%) as the runners up (Miljøstyrelsen 2017). 
The report “Potential for Denmark as a Circular Economy (…)” developed by the Ellen MacArt-
hur Foundation  (2015b, p.14), shows significant potential for growth related to CE and the report 
estimates that by 2035 CE initiatives could contribute 0.8-1.4% to GDP along with 7000-13000 
new jobs. The report also states that such a transition will not happen by itself as many barri-
ers exist that needs to be addressed: Inadequately defined legal frameworks; immature techno-
logy; customs and habits, plus capabilities and skills in the industry (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 
2015b). The Danish findings in many ways match those of the European manufacturing sector, 
with several challenges: lack of skilled labour, high resource dependency and low adoption of new 
technologies (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 2015a).
During the summer in 2017 the Danish Government’s ‘Advisory Board on Circular Economy’ 
finalised and handed in its 27 recommendations to the government on how to increase initiatives 
within reducing, reusing, recycling, but most importantly how to rethink systems and inventions 
(Besenbacher 2017). The recommendations address a large set of barriers and market drivers to 
set an ambitious circular goal for Denmark to release the CE potential. 
Listed below are a few of the relevant recommendations regarding the scope of this research:
#2. “Set up a single point of  entry to the authorities for enterprises that encounter barri-
ers to circular transition”
As companies innovate to transform their business they are more likely to encounter regulatory 
barriers that are not equipped to deal with companies working across existing value chains. The 
==PAGE:26==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 8
goal is to facilitate and streamline the evolvement of regulation that obstruct new circular solu-
tions.
#10. “Strengthen circular product policy” 
An aim to shift product policy focus from energy consumption in the use phase, to look at a bro-
ader focus on the overall impact of the product, in line with the EU Eco-Design directive. This 
shift would incentivise companies to look more to repair, disassembly, reuse and recycling in their 
product design.
#11. “Incorporate circular economy into manufacturing industry”
More direct promotion of CE to SME’s to help with the lack of skills, tools and know-how in 
implementing CE.  
#12. “Create a circular Building Regulation”
Set requirements for all new buildings and large-scale renovations that will lead to a sustainability 
classification class, this should lead to a greater focus on circular economy in buildings during 
their lifetime. 
#22. “Promote repair and reuse”
Demand better consumer information from companies on the expected lifetime of products and 
spare part availability, this along with a simpler and more clever use of VAT schemes that could 
support the business models of more long-lived products.
1.1.6 The MATChE platform 
The MATChE platform seeks to demonstrate how manufacturing companies can engage in the 
Circular Economy transition, primarily through a readiness assessment that clarifies their current 
readiness level as well as strengths and opportunities. By supporting manufacturing companies 
on their journey towards CE, the aim of MATChE is, hopefully, to increase circular businesses 
in Denmark.

==PAGE:27==

page 9
MATChE stands for MAking the Transition to Circular Economy and is an initiative carried out 
by a research team in the Department of Mechanical Engineering at the Technical University of 
Denmark in order to support Danish industry in the transition to a Circular Economy. MATChE 
is funded by The Danish Industry Foundation and is a collaboration project with The Confe-
deration of Danish Industry, CHORA Connection and The Danish Environmental Protection 
Agency who all stand for various activities.  
The MATChE.dk platform consist of four different elements functioning as steps towards CE: 
Knowledge Universe, Readiness Assessment, Accelerator Programme and Experience Sharing. 
In these four steps the function of MATChE is to, respectively, inspire, educate, challenge and 
engage Danish companies’ journey in transitioning to Circular Economy: 
 -
The Knowledge Universe consists of relevant material related to Circular Economy with 
the purpose of providing the companies with basic knowledge and understandings.
 -
The Readiness Assessment is accessible through creating a profile on my.MATChE.com 
and the main activity on the platform with the purpose of screening how ready manufactu-
ring companies are for Circular Economy by identifying strengths and opportunities based 
on the readiness score.
 -
The Accelerator Programme is a programme which companies can sign up for with the 
purpose of developing types of transition routes based on those pilot projects.
 -
The Experience Sharing community is meant for stakeholders, engaged into smaller Clu-
ster Circles, to share thoughts and experience regarding best practices, ideas and challenges 
aiming for a collaboration to transition from a linear to a circular model. These Cluster Circles 
are based on industry sectors so that the challenges and opportunities within those are related. 
The Eight Dimensions
The MATChE assessment is arranged around 8 dimensions of CE that broadly covers circular 
stages and is relevant to the manufacturing industry: 
 -
Organisation: measures the internal business capabilities of the company in order to imple-
ment new initiatives, such as CE. 
 -
Strategy and Business Model Innovation: measures the capabilities to enable long-term 
strategies to be developed with focus on business model innovation. 
 -
Product and Service Innovation: measures the capabilities required in the development of 
new solutions suitable in a CE context. 
 -
Manufacturing and Value Chain: measures the capabilities that can aid the companies in 
creating new value chain engagements and partnerships for maximum value creation of re-
sources. 
 -
Technology and Data: measures the company’s capabilities for the creation of value, th-
rough enhanced data management and sharing solutions.
 -
Use, Support and Maintenance: measures the capabilities needed to provide enhanced 
maintenance and repair services for an extension of value creation of solutions. 
 -
Takeback and End-of-Life Strategies: measures the capabilities that will ensure maximised 
==PAGE:28==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 10
value of the company’s end-of-use products. 
 -
Policy and Market: measures the external readiness of the legislative frameworks and mar-
kets in regard to development and provision of circular initiatives. 
Figure 7 – MATChE Dimensions are illustrated in a hexagon central for the readiness assessment (from MATChE.dk)
Those eight dimensions derives from clustering of the 30 success factors (see Appendix 2) the 
companies are yet to answer in the assessment, that again are based on research in best practices, 
success, - and case-stories related to CE. The authors have illustrated in Appendix 3 how the 
MATChE dimensions relate to the iResolve actions, a framework originally developed by EMF 
and McKinsey (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 2015a, p.26), and further extended with the ‘I’ for 
Implementation in the article “Integrating Backcasting and Eco-Design for the Circular Economy 
- The BECE Framework” by Mendoza et al (2017). 
The Readiness Scale 
MATChE measures the companies’ readiness for Circular Economy on a readiness scale going 
from not being ready to ready. The scale is:
 -
Understand potential 
 -
Plan Pilot Implementation
 -
Piloting Initiatives
 -
Plan Scale-Up
 -
Scale Initiative
The authors have illustrated in Appendix 3 how this readiness scale relates to other definitions 
of process steps used by the other CE platforms Circular Design Guide (Ellen MacArthur Foun-
dation & Ideo 2018), ResCom (Ellen MacArthur Foundation et al. 2018) and SHIFT (MIT et al. 
2018).

Clusters Circles 
MATChE wishes to create cluster circles that are smaller communities where challenges, best 
practices and ideas can flourish to find common solutions for CE transition. These clusters are 
based on sector codes in the idea of creating a coherence in the challenges and opportunities 
experienced by the companies within each cluster. The cluster circles are:
 -
Cluster circle 1: Food, Textile and Paper.
 -
Cluster circle 2: Pharma, Chemistry and Plastic. 
 -
Cluster circle 3: Transport, Machinery, Metal and Electronic. 
 -
Cluster circle 4: Furniture and Buildings. 
Assessing readiness levels and benchmarking
The readiness assessment is possible through company registration on my.MATChE.dk. A com-
pany profile can be connected with an unlimited number of employees. 
 -
First step is to identify the scope for the assessment; either by choosing the whole company 
level or a specific business unit level, e.g. user’s own department. 
 -
The assessment is then taken by answering the 30 questions distributed between the 8 dimen-
sions for readiness.
 -
A total Readiness Score for the selected scope will then be generated. If the scope chosen is 
the whole company, the results will be generated based on the consolidated Readiness Asses-
sments submitted by employees connected to the specific company profile.
The assessment and the user relation to MATChE is meant to be an iterative process as compani-
es advance their readiness towards CE. Therefore, it is possible to return and update the answers. 
Strengths and opportunities are identified for the chosen scope based on the score followed by a 
report outlining current status and recommendations for taking the next step. 
MATChE, being an open source platform, also enables the possibility to benchmark one’s own 
results in relation to other companies that relates e.g. in sector, size etc. Appendix 3 includes an 
elaborated introduction to MATChE through graphic material. 
1.2 Need for initiatives on the micro level 
Even though that there is an increase in the belief and evidence of circular production systems 
and these systems being beneficial not only in relation to the environment, but also in relation 
to the profit and prosperity of businesses, the knowledge on how to implement this on the mi-
cro level is scarce (Franco 2017; Linder et al. 2017; Elia et al. 2017). The lack of knowledge and 
==PAGE:30==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 12
support can also be seen in answers to the survey about CE in Danish companies by The Danish 
Environmental Protection Agency (2017), where the participating companies mention lack of 
knowledge and support as the main barrier to implementing CE in their company (37 percent).  
Lieder and Rashid (2016) suggest that more research should be done to support the Bottom-up 
side of the framework. The overall purpose of MATChE is in line with this goal of suppor-
ting the implementation and transition of Danish manufacturing companies to CE. As stated in 
Chapter 2 it is important to research transition on the micro level - or create visibility of existing 
research. 
In order to develop support, two ways are recognized as creating a meaningful impact:
 -
Develop a support that can aid the CE transition. 
 -
Develop a support that provides an overview of the existing tools available today.
In order to develop the first, we need an overview of existing solutions and areas where soluti-
ons are lacking. Therefore, this thesis will focus on increasing the availability of current material, 
support companies in the identification of tools and ensuring the expansion of the knowledge 
domain by identifying those areas that are not as well-researched or implementable for companies.
The motivation behind choosing the second option is further described to bridge the gap between 
research on micro-level transition and the manufacturing industry by moving the results of re-
search within circular tools into the network of manufacturing companies. This will make the re-
search function as a boundary object between the two networks instead of only being available for 
the academic network. Boundary objects are characterized by being ambiguous in the sense that 
they are something that can be communicated across networks, but still need to be specific and 
have a sufficient degree of meaningfulness within each of the networks (Star & Griesemer 1989, 
p.393). This means that boundary objects have a duality; on the one hand, they act as ‘bridge buil-
ders’ between collaborative networks and on the other hand act as separators of these networks. 
Figure 8 - Bridge the gap by moving research into industries 
That bridge is interesting, since this is where the translation of the boundary object can be found, 
and in relation to this project, how the translation is happening and can be shaped in order for the 
bridge to function and be used.

 2.1 Design Research Methodology
The chosen methodology for this thesis is the Design Research Methodology (DRM) by Blessing 
& Chakrabarti (2009). This methodology has been chosen due to the findings in the previous 
section and the motivation behind using the DRM methodology is that design research tends to 
lack the following according to Blessing & Chakrabarti (2009): an overview of existing research, 
implementation and impact of research results and scientific rigour. With emphasis on the third 
issue, the authors propose DRM to support a design research process aiming to address all of the 
three issues cf.:
“Design research must be scientific in order for the results to have validity in 
some generic, theoretical as well as practical sense. For this, design research has to develop and 
validate knowledge systematically.” (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009, p.9)
It has been applied as the overall framework for the design research and has also provided guide-
lines for the overall planning and scoping of the project as well as the specific phase activities. 
Hence, the thesis consists of four partly parallel and iterative main stages: Research Clarification 
(RS), Descriptive Study I (DS 1), Prescriptive Study (PS) and Descriptive Study II (DS 2). Figure 
9 illustrates the links between the stages as well as inputs and outputs for each stage: 
Figur 9 - DRM Methodology. Reproduction of ﬁgure from Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009.

Even though the process is illustrated in a linear fashion, this is not the case in reality, where the 
process is influenced by parallel execution of the stages and many iterations. Blessing & Chakra-
barti has noted that while methodology should help realise a better planned research process and 
thereby enhance the chance of obtaining valid and suitable results, even though that cannot be 
ensured, the methodology can only support the process. It should be used in a flexible way in 
order to allow iterations (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009, p.14). Especially the two first stages, RC 
and DS-1, have been an overlapping and iterative process in this process. How the DRM stages 
relates to the phases of this thesis can be seen in Figure 10.

Figur 10. How the DRM stages have covered the thesis process
According to Blessing & Chakrabarti the objective of a design research process is two-part; de-
velopment of understanding and development of support. The understanding is developed th-
rough formulation and validation of theories and models about the phenomenon of design. The 
support is developed and validated on these theories and models with the aim of improving the 
design; both in process and product of this. The support in itself can take any form: the content 
of the solution is facilitated to the users through a medium. 
Figure 11. Support consists of both form & medium
==PAGE:36==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 18
As suggested by Blessing & Chakrabarti, it is not expected that a research project includes all 
steps or that each of the steps are carried out in equal depth. Therefore, they proceed to suggest 
seven possible types of design research determined by the scope of the project against available 
time and resources. Due to this research being limited to the time constraint as a master thesis, 
this research has been planned and executed as a Type 3 research focusing on the comprehensive 
development of support. Meaning that the content of the first two stages (RC & DS 1) has been 
review-based, the third stage (PS) has been performed comprehensively and the content in the last 
stage (DS 2) has been done on a very initial basis in order to prepare the results for use by others. 
It has been sufficient to perform the first two stages as review-based because the knowledge and 
assumptions given by the supervisor team, based on the research behind the MATChE project, 
provided enough evidence for the initial understanding of the research area. Furthermore, and 
identified in the RC stage, the literature indicates that there is a need to develop a support that 
can enhance the visibility of CE tools and create an overview of existing research (Blessing & 
Chakrabarti 2009, p.61). Therefore, no empirical study was performed in DS-1, but instead it was 
chosen to focus directly on the development of the support, both as part of DS-1 with the syste-
matisation of tools and in the PS stage. 
 2.2 Scoping the research: Setting system      
.boundaries
Identification of the overall topic of interest and choice of area of research (Blessing & Chakra-
barti 2009) is partly based on the project brief given by the supervisors of this thesis; Tim McA-
loone and Daniela Pigosso, who are part of the MATChE team, and the initial understanding 
obtained and described through the previous chapters:
Issue of  interest: Circular Economy Transition
Activity and/or stage of  the design process: A support tool that can help with identification 
of relevant tools in relation to the results from a prior assessment. The development of this sup-
port will be in content and embodiment
Area of  application: Manufacturing companies. The support tool will be developed in a Danish 
context, but with the possibility of a broader application. 
Since the overall topic has been pre-defined prior to the authors’ work, the thesis is also based on 
==PAGE:37==

research carried out by the team behind MATChE and in continuation of this, already validated 
as a qualified research area, both in terms of being academically and practically worthwhile (Bles-
sing & Chakrabarti 2009, p.43). Regarding the research as being realistic, scoping has been done 
both by authors in collaboration with Tim and Daniela and as a continual part of the project. 
Delimitations and limitations of work conducted in each stage will be presented continuously in 
the respective sections. 
MATChE has focus on Danish manufacturing companies, and this target has therefore been ap-
plied to this thesis as well. However, this does not mean that the support tool may not be relevant 
to manufacturing companies in other countries - or that it does not contain tools that can be used 
by users beyond this focus. 
The attention of the research has been to form the next step on the user-journey from the MAT-
ChE assessment. The focus for the development of the support solution has therefore been to 
enable identification and potential selection of tools based on the user’s readiness assessment. 
Thus, the solution delimits the facilitation of tools and the actual implementation. This is also 
reflected in the final set of articles and related tools: these are selected on the basis of a systematic 
process and analysis based on different criteria. Thus, a further qualitative assessment and content 
analysis has not taken place, other than the one already performed during the systematic literature 
review.
 2.3 Research aims & objectives
The aim of this thesis is to establish an overview of existing tools for circular economy and 
support companies in the identification of tools according to their objectives and readiness level. 
In order to achieve this aim, it has been deployed into the specific objectives below: 
 
Research Clarification (RC) – Literature review: 
 -
Establish an initial understanding of the research area and design a protocol to be used in the 
Systematic Literature Review 
Descriptive Study 1 (DS-1) – Identification and Systematisation of  tools for CE: 
 -
Conduct a Systematic Literature Review to find relevant tools within different dimensions of 
Circular Economy related to the manufacturing industry
 -
Systematize these tools in a “backstage” framework to allow different user approaches and 
enable tool suggestions on the MATChE platform. 
Prescriptive Study (PS) – Development of  support tool:

 2.4 Description of the four stages 
The following parts briefly describe each stage of the thesis both in regard to the theoretical met-
hodology and the different thesis activities undertaken in the specific stage. 
 2.4.1 Research Clariﬁcation – Literature Review 
The purpose of this stage is to clarify the current understanding and the overall research aim, de-
velop a research plan and provide a focus for the following stages (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009, 
p.15). The Research Clarification stage (RC) can proceed concurrently with some exploratory 
study (DS-I) in order to develop the research goals and plan of the overall project, when the un-
derstanding about the research topic is not fully developed. The aims of this stage are to identify 
and refine a research problem that is both academically and practically worthwhile and realistic, 
among other things, by undertaking an exploratory review of the literature in order to identify to 
which extent identified challenges are already solved in practice or academia, and what potentially 
still needs to be solved. 
This stage is already presented in the introduction in the previous section, where a research fra-
mework was established both based on the project brief and by reviewing literature concerning 
the relevant research areas. This was done with an aim to identify and explore possible gaps in 
the implementation of CE. As a result, the aims and objectives of concrete research, followed 
by success criteria, were determined. The RC stage proved helpful to establish an understanding 
of Circular Economy and the complexity of transitions and subsequently in revealing the overall 
research aim. 
2.4.2 Descriptive Study 1 – Identiﬁcation and Systematisation of Tools for CE
The aim of the Descriptive Study I (DS-1) stage is to increase the understanding of the existing 
situation and influencing factors that determine success (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009). This stage 
==PAGE:39==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 21
compromises two phases of the project, namely; the identification of tools for CE and the sy-
stematisation of the tools, in order to develop the backstage framework. Both phases have been 
performed in order to develop the form of the support tool. The result of this stage is which 
factors that are most suitable to address in the Prescriptive Study in order for improvement. The 
main methodology used in DS-1 has been the Systematic Literature Review (SLR) and a biblio-
metric analysis has been performed on the results from the SLR. While this stage took place, a 
refinement of the Research Clarification was conducted at the same time, when introduced to 
additional literature through the SLR.  
 2.4.2.1 Systematic Literature Review
Literature reviews are usually the initial step in any research or development initiative and from a 
scientific perspective a compulsory step (Biolchini et al. 2005, p.2). Systematic Reviews are a re-
view of a clearly formulated question that uses systematic and explicit methods to identify, select 
and critically appraise relevant research, and to collect and analyse data from the records obtained 
in the review (Higgins & Green 2008)
There are several advantages in a systematisation and documentation of the process. According to 
Biolchini et al. (2005, p.3), one of the advantages is that a systematic approach aims to determine a 
more formal and organized process of conducting this type of investigation and by that avoiding 
the latent biases of the unsystematic review. Another important aspect mentioned is that the who-
le process is explicitly defined in order to enable other professionals to reproduce the protocol or 
evaluate the adequacy of the design and execution (Biolchini et al. 2005, p.1). Subsequently, syste-
matic reviews enable the identification of as many relevant records possible within resource limits. 
Executing a systematic literature review (SLR) consists of three steps as developed and demon-
strated in (Biolchini et al, 2005) and depicted in Figure 12:
1) Planning, which includes specifying: research objectives, a review protocol and developing a 
search string.
2) Execution, which involves primary records identification, selection and evaluation based    on 
the inclusion and exclusion criteria established in the review protocol. 
3) Result Analysis of the extracted information. 
Figure 12 -  SLR process illustrated. Reproduction of ﬁgure from Biolchini et al, 2005. 
==PAGE:40==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 22
 2.4.2.2 Bibliometric analysis
A bibliometric analysis enables the observation of the state of art through the overall production 
of scientific literature, at a given level of specialisation. It has become a common term for a va-
riety of approaches focused at quantifying scientific research regarding the amount of published 
literature, collaboration patterns and impact characteristics (Okubo 1997).
 2.4.2.3 Classiﬁcation Criteria 
The second phase involved the systematisation of the tools by classification criteria. This syste-
matisation was performed in two steps:
Step 2.1: Definition of classification criteria to facilitate the users’ identification of relevant tools. 
This was done both through ideation by the authors and in collaboration with the MATChE team. 
Both processes with focus on the companies’ needs.  
Step 2.2: Classification of the tools, found in the SLR, according to the final criteria. 
The classification in this thesis resulted in the backstage framework – a database which makes it 
possible to filter tools based on several different classifications. 
 2.4.3  Prescriptive Study – Support tool development 
The Prescriptive Study stage starts with the results from RC and DS-I and the aim is to develop 
the design support, a prescription (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009, p.144). This support is not di-
rectly derived from the findings from the previous stages; it is a task itself with a starting point 
in divergent and explorative thinking with a use of different problem solving and development 
methods.
The Prescriptive Study in this thesis has been performed comprehensively in the sense that it has 
been realised to such an extent that the functionality could be evaluated. The level of complete-
ness of the support is scoped regarding to the constraints of time and resources, which means 
that the support is developed only as a prototype and for evaluation in a test environment. 
 2.4.3.1 Double diamond
The Double Diamond Model (Design Council 2018) illustrates the overall phases of a design 
project and is characterized by its changing diverging and converging phases (see Figure 13). 
Within these distinct phases different activities happen to support the aims: discovering, defining, 
developing, and delivering. The model has been used as a mindset for the PS stage in order to 
synthesise towards a final concept for the support. Several diverging and converging activities 
and methods were used during the PS stage. Two primary methods used are described below, the 
==PAGE:41==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 23
remainder of which will be described in Section 4. 
Figur 13: Double Diamond (Figure based on Design Council 2018)
 2.4.3.2 Co-creation 
Co-creation refers to any act of collective creativity and is a broad term with plentiful applications 
and the practice of collective creativity originates in the participatory design movement of the 
1970s. (Sanders & Stappers 2008). Co-creation is another example of a concept called an umbrella 
term, as described with CE in part 2.2. One description is that co-creation happens when users 
step into the shoes of designers, and are provided with tools to be able to generate new ideas and 
are facilitated in the creative session by designers and researchers (Mattelmäki & Visser 2011). The 
co-creation session can be planned in various degrees, dependent on the purpose. Co-creation 
has, in this thesis, been used as part of a workshop held with a Danish textile service company. 
 2.4.3.3 Prototyping 
Prototyping is to give form to an idea, evaluate it and improve upon it. The mindset behind using 
prototypes are “fail fast” – by making the ideas tangible, the sooner we are able to converge tow-
ards a solution (Brown & Katz 2009). A prototype is an unfinished and, often, physical test versi-
on of a product, service, or process and can be used to develop, test and communicate ideas and 
concepts. As the process evolves and ideas are selected and specified, typically fewer and more 
refined prototypes are used. Prototyping has, in this thesis, been used with the aim of embodying 
the support in such manner that it could mediate the concept and could be used for evaluation in 
the DS-2 stage. 
 2.4.4  Descriptive Study 2 – Evaluation and test of the Support tool 
The final stage of this thesis is the Descriptive Study 2, in which, Blessing and Chakrabarti (2009) 
states the aim is to evaluate and measure the impact of the support tool. The evaluation of the 
intended support is twofold: both in regard to application and success. An Application evaluation 
==PAGE:42==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 24
seeks to identify whether the support can be used for the task for which it is intended, whereas a 
Success evaluation is involved continuously during development and seeks to identify whether the 
intended impact of the support has been realised (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009, p.181). 
An initial DS-2 has been performed in this thesis due to the project scope and duration. An initial 
DS-2 is argued by Blessing and Chakrabarti (2009, p.195) to be required in research projects, in 
order for the researchers to be able to draw conclusions about the relation between the developed 
support and research aims of the project. The focus of an initial DS-2 is the Application evalua-
tion and seeks to give an indication of applicability, usability and usefulness of the support. 
The tests and evaluation conducted in this thesis have been based on the established success cri-
teria and linked measurable success criteria stated in the following section 2.5. Type and design of 
tests will be further explained in Chapter 5. 
 2.5 Success criteria 
Formulating criteria for success is necessary in order to be capable to determine whether the so-
lution helped to achieve the aim of the research (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009, p.26). Two overall 
success criteria have been articulated to later enable the evaluation of the outcome of the support 
development. Since there is no established metrics to measure ‘success’, they are followed by a 
measurable success criterion that will be applied for the evaluation in Chapter 5. 
Success criteria 1: Ability to identify tools
 -
Measurable success criteria: Is it useful? 
Success criteria 2: Ability to comprehend value of tools 
 -
Measurable success criteria: Is it usable?

The result is a backstage framework that supports the filtering of tools 
across different classifications. In section 3.1.1.5, an analysis of research

 3.1 Results of the Descriptive Stage 1 
 3.1.1 Phase 1: Identiﬁcation of tools for Circular Economy 
The main part of Phase 1 has been to plan and execute a SLR. This has been part of this project 
for numerous reasons. First and foremost, because CE is a broad concept and covers an enor-
mous amount of material. A systematic planning and execution enables a relevant scoping of the 
literature review resulting in equally relevant material. Furthermore, Circular Economics is a field 
of rapid development. Therefore, it is highly relevant to document the process so the solution 
for the MATChE platform, based on the SLR, can be continuously developed and evaluated by 
other professionals and thereby maintain its relevance. Different methods of evaluation were 
recommended by the method. These were used between each step in order to move to the next 
step (Biolchini et al. 2005, p.17). Throughout this SLR the evaluation between each step has both 
consisted of evaluation by experts and by testing. The experts here are the supervisors for this 
thesis: Professor Tim C. McAloone and Senior Researcher Daniela Cristina Antelmi Pigosso.
This section presents the results found during the SLR, which contained identification of tools 
for CE transformation. The results of each step: Planning, Execution and Analysis will be descri-
bed with a reflection of the process in the end.
 3.1.1.1 Step 1. Planning 
The planning step of a SLR is an important part of scoping the research. The aim of the Syste-
matic Literature Review Protocol (see Appendix 4) is to guide the execution of a SLR by defining 
the main focus of the research, questions and objectives formularisation and criteria used throug-
hout to identify, evaluate, include and analyse records (Biolchini et al. 2005). The main goal of 
this systematic review was to establish an overview of the state of art of tools for CE transition 
with the perspective of enabling the development of an updateable database. And the objective 
for this SLR has been: 
What tools are present that aid the transition towards a Circular Economy in the scope of  Danish manufacturing 
companies?
A selection of target articles and references, indicated by the expert Daniela Pigosso, were chosen 
to form a set of primary studies (see Appendix 5). These were both important in order for the 
authors to familiarise themselves with the methodology of systematic literature review and the 
theory of Circular Economy and was subsequently used for the development of the SLR pro-
tocol, especially regarding the definition of keywords and strings relevant to the search. 
It is worth mentioning that the development of this protocol has been a very iterative process that 
==PAGE:47==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 29
has happened parallel to the scoping of the project, understanding of the context has increased 
and tests have highlighted the need for improvement of the protocol. To limit the search results 
to a manageable size within the scope of the thesis, several iterations were tested as to arrive at a 
pool of articles to be manually assessed. In particular, the development of keywords and criteria 
has been a continuous process parallel with the execution of the protocol, as it became clear what 
to include and exclude based on the results.
The organisation and systemisation has taken place in Mendeley (Reference manager) and in 
Google Sheets. This will be further described in section 3.1.1.2 about the execution of the SLR. 
3.1.1.1.1 Deﬁning Keywords 
The inertial set of keywords was compiled based on the primary set of studies, MATChE plat-
form dimensions and prior knowledge from CE relevant courses during the design and innova-
tion engineering education. Primary studies were: Blomsma & Brennan 2017; Korhonen et al. 
2018; Kirchherr et al. 2017; Murray et al. 2017, which have assisted as reference and inspiration 
in defining the keywords for the search string. Relevant keywords were further elaborated with 
relevant synonyms. The development of a final search-string has also consisted of iterations (see 
Appendix 6).
The decision was made to structure the search-string into three levels using the Boolean Opera-
tors “AND” and “OR“: 
A: Keywords related to the main focus i.e. “Circular Economy”
B: Keywords related to tools and methods 
C: Keywords related directly to the 8 MATChE Readiness Dimensions
An initial search was made prior to expert evaluation to get an overview of the amount of results. 
The need for limiting the literature review through keywords became clear. An example hereof 
was how the C keyword “waste management”, a synonym based on the dimension “take-back 
and end-of-life”, resulted close to 200 results, most of them with a low relevance to the target 
group for MATChE. Keywords with similar results were removed. In addition, words related to 
CE (A-level words) were removed. 
An initial search-string based on 3 A level-, 10 B level- and 69 C level- keywords was then evalua-
ted by Professor Tim C. McAloone and Senior Researcher Daniela Cristina Antelmi Pigosso (Ap-
pendix 6), resulting in a revised search-string compiled of 2 A-level keywords, 6 B-level keywords 
and 36 C-level keywords. See Figure 14 for the full search-string.

A consideration was to execute the search with eight different search-strings based on the 8 di-
mensions, i.e. 8 different searches. However, the choice became one overall search string to avoid 
the challenge within overlapping material.
3.1.1.1.2 
Deﬁning sources
The objective is here to select where searches for records will be executed. The selection of sour-
ces was divided into the two scientific search databases, Scopus and Web of Science, with presen-
ce of search mechanisms using keyword. The selection of sources was chosen in collaboration 
with Tim and Daniela. Also, sources within grey literature was defined, if it proved necessary to 
execute within grey literature. See Appendix 4 for final list of sources. 
To verify the sources and the search-string some initial searches were made in Scopus and Web 
==PAGE:49==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 31
of Science (WoS). Early test searches gave indications of a high level of overlapping between the 
two search databases, around 80%. Due to the amount of manual work involved in identifying 
duplicates, only Scopus was used in the search optimisation process and the results were multi-
plied by 1.2 to give an estimate of the final article count. The initial search in Scopus returned 592 
articles, estimating 710 as a total count. To estimate the manual workload in evaluating the articles 
in relation to the inclusion-exclusion criteria, a test run evaluation was carried out on a selection 
of articles and five minutes per article was established as an achievable time, giving an estimated 
total workload of 59 hours.   
To optimise the search for relevance in the scientific databases, only title, abstract and keywords 
were searched, the output was limited to articles and book chapters, and due to language barriers 
only in English (using English search terms already limited this to only a few items). 
3.1.1.1.3 
Deﬁning records selection 
Once the sources are defined, it is needed to develop the criteria and the process for how studies 
are selected and evaluated. 
The first step is to define the criteria on which basis the records are included or excluded. This is 
an essential part of an SLR because a search executed in web engines may result in a great number 
of articles that do not reply to the research question (Biolchini et al. 2005, p.12). 
As mentioned earlier in section 3.1.1.1 the development of those criteria has taken place in the 
planning phase but also as part of the executing concurrent with the review of records.  
Normally criteria such as number of citations, impact factor of the journal and other quantities 
can be used in the selection, but since CE is such an emerging field, this did not seem relevant or 
sufficient in this SLR and therefore the criteria are based on a more qualitative content analysis. 
The final set of criteria were as following: 
Final inclusion criteria:
1. Subject area must be of relevance for the Danish manufacturing industry i.e. relevant for the 
MATChE target users.
2. Focus of article should be on Micro- or Meso- level, i.e. company or regional level.
3. Article must present a prescriptive tool/method/guide to solve a strategic, practical or imple-
mentational problem.
4. The presented tool must be validated through at least a case study. 
5. A tool specific to a technology or area should show potential for application on a more ge-
neral matter. 
Final exclusion criteria:
1. Subject area out of scope. 
2. If the focus of the article is on a macro level.
3. The article concerns the topic in a descriptive manner. 
==PAGE:50==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 32
4. Geographically out of scope.
5. Articles that only show the implementation of a well-known tool and the results thereof.
6. Article information is not presented in a succinct manner i.e. clearly and understandably com-
municated. 
7. If the article is too narrowly focused on a specific sector or technology. 
8. If the method is a too narrowly focused area. 
Development of  inclusion criteria:
 -
Inclusion criteria 1 and 3 were developed based on the scope of the thesis. 
 -
Inclusion criteria 2 and 4 were developed with the aim of enabling application and use of 
the tool within the target users of MATChE. 
 -
Inclusion criterion 5 was developed continuously with the execution as an opportunity to 
include tools that would otherwise be excluded based on exclusion criteria 7 and 8. 
Development of  exclusion criteria:
 -
Exclusion criteria 1, 2 and 3 were developed as a counterpart to the first three inclusion 
criteria. It was useful to have the criteria articulated as both an inclusion and exclusion criteria 
for the SLR process documentation. 
The remaining criteria were developed continuously with the execution when there was a need for 
these to exclude articles that otherwise lived up to the inclusion criteria:
 -
Exclusion criterion 4 was developed to exclude tools that were based or heavily dependent 
on a specific national context, one that was not Denmark, primarily in relation to legislation.
 -
Exclusion criterion 5 was developed based on research delimitation. The need for this cri-
terion, however, occurred continuously as more records presented tools that can be defined 
as ‘well-known’ e.g. Life Cycle Assessments. This criterion enabled a faster screening and it 
is argued that these well-known tools could easily be implemented as part of the support in 
the future. 
 -
Exclusion criterion 6 was developed as an option for ongoing quality evaluation. 
 -
Exclusion criteria 7 and 8 were developed to ensure a wide application of the tool to avoid 
reverse irrelevance for a majority of users.
Both the inclusion and exclusion criteria were used throughout all selection procedures. To be 
selected, a study should contain all the inclusion criteria defined above. 
Selection procedure
In order to evaluate the inclusion criteria in each study, the following three studies selection pro-
cedures were defined in the protocol. 
 -
Studies selection procedure 1 (SP#1): Reading of the title, keywords and abstract. 
 -
Studies selection procedure 2 (SP#2): If this proved insufficient for detecting the inclusi-
on criteria, the introduction and conclusion were read. 
 -
Studies selection procedure 3 (SP#3): If necessary, the study was fully read. 
==PAGE:51==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 33
The protocol includes a paragraph about how the selected records were stored and recorded for 
each Selection Procedure and how the analysis should be done (see Appendix 4).

 3.1.1.3 Step 3. Analysis 
The included studies were synthesized by extracting the relevant information combined with a 
bibliometric analysis. In this research, the bibliometric method has been used with the purpose 
of highlighting potential research gaps both relevant for reflection of the SLR process as well as 
supporting MATChE's research perspective. 
This analysis will therefore seek to answer:
Where can the bibliometric analysis illuminate weaknesses in the SLR process and where can the reason be lack 
of  research? 
Obviously, it is difficult to define a definitive answer as to whether the reason is due to one or the 
other and therefore the analysis is based on a reflexive approach.
3.1.1.3.1 
Bibliometric results
The bibliometric results obtained in this research is to be presented below in Table 1. The articles 
for the included tools were analysed using Google Sheets and ScienceScape’s bibliometric tools 
(ScienceScape 2018).

Number of  papers per year
The research within CE is in rapid development and 51% of the included papers were published 
only 14 months prior to the search, and 91% are published within the last 24 months (see Figure 
17). 
Figure 17: Bibliometric analysis - Number of papers per year (Final set of studies)
We recognise the same trend when looking at all 453 papers returned from the search string where 
an almost exponential growth is visible; showing the rapid expansion of CE and growing interest 
in the field (see Figure 18).
The numbers also empathize how young the CE field is, which can have an impact on how con-
solidated the knowledge is and, in this case, how mature the found tools are. To find more tools 
to support a CE transition there could be a need to consider other fields where more consolidated 
practices could support the aim of CE.
Among authors in the included studies, only nine appear in more than one study (see Table 2). 
This could indicate that CE is not the main research field for the authors, but since several of the 
authors are considered to be prominent in the field, this does not apply. 
Therefore, a limiting factor could be due to the novelty of CE or that the design of the SLR dis-
turbs the overall picture. This could for example be because the paper must show a tool meaning 
that descriptive papers from the authors could have been excluded.

3.1.1.3.2 
Summarising the bibliometric analysis
Looking at the authors, journals and keywords data it could indicate a multidisciplinary research 
field, where many researchers, from different research areas, engage and contribute with specific 
knowledge by relating it to CE. Due to the small sample size of only 47 studies that cover a broad 
subject area, it should also be noted that it is difficult to transfer the results from here to a more 
general picture and why some of the findings may therefore be biased of this.

 3.1.1.4 Scoping of the SLR 
The planning of the literature review has been scoped several times in the attempt to generate a 
result that was possible to review and systematise, both during the actual planning phase and as 
iterations in form of tests of the execution. The SLR comprised just published articles and book 
chapters and the final execution was not iterated. 
The keywords related to Circular Economy were heavily reduced through the development of 
the SLR protocol. As previously mentioned in the introduction in Chapter 1, it is impossible to 
trace Circular Economy back to a single concept. Instead it is a generic concept and its practical 
application to economic systems and industrial processes has been developed in the academic, 
political and industrial domains by different schools of thoughts such as cradle-to-cradle, perfor-
mance economy, regenerative design, etc. (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 2018). There will there-
fore, potentially, be many useful tools related to other concepts that have not been included in 
this research, but as a delimitation; and to an extend a limitation, the keywords were focused on 
this particular concept and this refinement has proved very positive in relation to both relevance 
of results and time delimitation of the research.
A method used to complement systematic literature reviews and to build upon the executed re-
sults is a snowballing approach. Snowballing refers to using the reference list of a paper or the 
citations to the paper to identify further records (Wohlin 2014). This could have been a way to 
ensure that the search string did not exclude relevant material. However, because the result at the 
given time would be reduced rather than increased in relation to the time span, this was not done.
Another delimitation of the project was that the research would confine itself to create an over-
view and systematise published literature from the beginning. Nevertheless, ‘Grey literature’, re-
search that is either unpublished or has been published in non-commercial form, was originally 
part of the SLR protocol. This was to ensure that if the result of execution showed major short-
comings within specific areas, a search for grey literature could cover this. At the same time, it 
could strengthen the support solution and therefore it is still relevant to perform this search, but 
this was not prioritised due to time.
 3.1.1.5 Identifying research gaps
The data extraction and classification of the tools in the Backstage Framework, yet to be descri-
bed in section 3.2.1, made it was possible to analyse how the tools related to the classification 
criteria with the aim of addressing potential gaps. 
From the first plot in Figure 19, it was clearly visible the SLR returned tools relevant in all eight 
MATChE dimensions.

Figure 19: Tool relevance in relation to MATChE dimensions 
Two dimensions stood out as only sparsely populated:
 -
“6 Technology and data” with four tools. 
 -
“5 Use, support and Maintenance” with only five tools.
Looking at sub-dimension level in Figure 20 three sub-dimensions stood out:
 -
“5.3 Sharing platforms” and “3.4 Design for Sharing” with no relevant tools identified.
 -
“5.1 Service Support” and “6.1 Monitor during use” both with only one tool being relevant 
to each.
