Possible reasons for the low number of tools that showed relevance in the dimensions could be 
related to the keywords used or the few sub-dimensions present within the dimension; two in “6 
-Technology and Data” and three in “5 - Use, support and Maintenance”. The former dimension 
was included in the search-string based on four keywords and the latter based on only 2. Com-
pared to the seventh dimension “Take-back and End-of-Life” that also had only three sub-di-
mensions, it still resulted in 20 relevant tools, which were represented with seven keywords in the 
search-string which potential could have influenced the result. However, this could also be due to 
a gap in research. 
 
Sub-dimensions with high coverage of tools were “3.3 Design for End-of-Life”, “2.1 New Value 
Proposition” and “2.2 Long-term Strategy” with between 19 and 24 tools found relevant.
When looking at implementation, the classification allows for the exploration of how the tools 
cover the different steps of the implementation process. From Figure 21 it is clear that most of 
the tools only deal with the beginning of the implementation process; understanding the potential 
and planning a pilot implementation. When moving on to the final steps of putting the plans into 
action, only half of the tools offer support and only 8 of 49 tools can help the user on the last 
step; scaling up initiatives. This lack of tools to bring projects the last step to implementation is 
worrying and may constitute a research gap. 
Figure 21: Tool relevance in relation to MATChE readiness scale
Other reasons for this could be again be ascribed to the fact that CE is a new concept and that the 
development of tools that can aid the companies in the transition is therefore focused on getting 
companies to understand the potential and planning pilot projects. Hopefully, as more companies 
advance and mature, more support tools will be developed to support the later part of the process 
and the companies themselves will inspire research and guide through case-stories. 
There was no significant finding about the distribution of tools in relation to clusters such as 
highly underrepresented sectors. Further results can be found in Appendix 11. 
Summarized, two possible research gaps were found:
 -
Lack of tools that support companies within the dimensions “6 - Technology and Data” and 
==PAGE:62==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 44
“5 - Use, Support and Maintenance”. 
 -
Lack of tools supporting the later stages in projects when going from pilot to scaling up 
initiatives. 
An iterated literature review with a broader set of keywords relating to those dimensions, or a 
search executed in grey literature, could uncover relevant tools valuable to companies. 
 3.1.2 Phase 2: Systematisation and development of backstage framework
The aim of the step was to extract the tools from the final set of studies and find characteristics 
across the articles that could be applied to the final tools to sort them, and thereby limit the num-
ber of suggested tools to just the ones relevant for the user in a specific context and to support 
their identification. 
The information extraction is normally, according to Biolchini et. al (2005), part of the execution 
process in the SLR, but it proved more efficient to run the information extraction concurrently 
with the classification since there will automatically be a data extraction of the relevant informa-
tion from the article in relation to the specific criterion. This data will be used in the Backstage 
Framework, that at the given time already contained simple information about the tools, which 
will be described in section 3.1.2.1. 
The classification criteria had to link up in a meaningful way with the MATChE assessment, so 
that the support tool could work in conjunction with the MATChE recommendations. The cri-
teria had to facilitate filtering, selection and communication of core information from the tool 
relevant for the user, to evaluate its value according to the companies’ needs. 
 3.1.2.1 Information Extraction 
Once the final set of primary studies was selected, the extraction of the tools began. All included 
articles were copied to a new spreadsheet with only the ID number, author and title exported. 
This spreadsheet was set up prior to the SP#3 reading of the last articles for evaluation, so that 
it was possible to extract information directly after the quality evaluation of a study could be ve-
rified. This enabled a faster process as the article was clear in memory after SP#3 and the article 
should not be read again afterwards to extract the tool. The process resulted in 49 tools identified. 
Two articles, (see articles with ID#7 and #104) resulted in 2 tools. All of the tools can be found 
in Appendix 12. 
A final retention rate against the initial numbers came to 10.37%. The retention rate could be 
useful to estimate new tools in future searches, a test of the search using the A+B+C string on 
Scopus, showed that between 27.02.2018 and 05.06.2018, 74 new articles were published. This 
would suggest that around 7 new tools could be added from this.
==PAGE:63==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 45
3.1.2.2 
Development of classiﬁcation criteria
Three of the classification criteria were based directly from the research behind MATChE proje-
ct. These are part of the MATChE readiness assessment: 
 -
Classification Criteria 1: Dimensions derived from the different groupings of the 30 suc-
cess factors, used in the MATChE assessment as questions (can be found in Appendix 2), that 
are described as sub-dimensions. The eight groupings of those are called dimensions.
 -
Classification Criteria 2: The readiness scale describes the process of implementing new 
initiatives in a manufacturing company.
 -
Classification Criteria 3: Clusters are the grouping of sectors into four cluster circles. These 
derives from MATChE and are meant to be smaller communities within the manufacturing 
industry that can have issues and opportunities in common. 
Along with these classification criteria, the name of the tool was recorded, and a short descriptive 
text was written about the tool. Other relevant metadata about the article, such as author, pub-
lication year, citations, abstract, keywords were linked to the entry by a unique id for future use.
The additional classification criteria were developed using input from: literature (Pigosso et al. 
2011), MATChE related knowledge, meeting with PhD student Marina de Pádua Pieroni and th-
rough meetings with supervisors Tim and Daniela. The ideation resulted in several classification 
criteria that were evaluated in Appendix 13 on three points:
 -
What: The aim of the criteria.
 -
Relevance: How the criteria was relevant to the user or project.
 -
How: Describing how the data could be retrieved from the article.
17 classification criteria were considered and a final set of 12 were selected to be added to the 
three existing ones (written in bold):
1. MATChE Dimension 
2. Readiness level of  tool (Application levels)
3. MATChE Cluster relevance
4. MATChE sub dimension 
5. Type of tool
6. User specialisation level (high/low)
7. Application and acquisition cost (high/low)
8. Demanded time for use (high/low)
9. Current development level of tool (theoretical/experimental/consolidated)
10. Input data (quantitative/qualitative)
11. Output data (quantitative/qualitative)
12. Information about the tool/method (succinct/superficial) 
Each of these criteria are described in the following part 3.1.2.3.  
==PAGE:64==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 46
 3.1.2.3 Description of ﬁnal classiﬁcation criteria 
This part describes the selected list of classification criteria, introducing their aims and guidelines 
on how to evaluate and classify.
Classification Criteria 1 & 4: MATChE Dimensions and Sub-dimensions
 -
Informing the user on where the tool can help support the transition to CE.
 -
Creating links between tools and the dimensions and thereby allows filtering of tools that 
could be used directly to autogenerate tools to user’s MATChE recommendations. 
 -
The reviewer must familiarize with the 30 MATChE questions and the concepts and te-
chnologies behind. If the tool is seen to support a sub-dimension, then it should be marked 
as relevant. Only sub-dimensions central to the tool should be marked relevant. If a sub-di-
mension is marked, then the overall dimension will, equally, be relevant. 
Classification Criteria 2: Readiness level of  tool (Application levels)
 -
Informing the user on where in the process of implementing new initiatives the tool can 
be of use. 
 -
Understanding where in the process the tool can add support can help and guide the user for 
the relevance of the tool for a given project.
 -
The reviewer must give an overall evaluation of the tool and mark the readiness levels that 
the tool can add support in:
1. Understanding the potential.
2. Planning pilot implementation.
3. Piloting initiatives.
4. Planning scale-up.
5. Scaling up initiatives.
Classification Criteria 3: MATChE Cluster relevance
 -
Identify tools that are either relevant or not for certain sectors, to be able to filter only tools 
that are relevant. The MATChE project has arranged sectors into four clusters and these clu-
sters were adapted as a simplified way of sorting. 
 -
The reviewer needs to evaluate if the tool has relevance for any of the sectors in a cluster. 
If relevant for one, then it should be deemed relevant for the cluster as a whole. This way of 
sorting is a compromise that will give some false positives in filtering: For example, where 
a food manufacturer can be presented with a tool relevant for textile or vice versa. But the 
idea of the clusters is built upon an idea where they will share some experience and proces-
ses, whereas it is important, as a reviewer, to think additional contexts into the tool if it is 
otherwise explicitly developed with a specific sector in mind. 
Classification Criteria 4: Type of  tool
 -
Identifying the type of tool could help prime the user on what type of support to expect 
from the tool.
 -
A list of tool types was compiled before classification. Types of tools were gathered from 
==PAGE:65==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 47
literature, meeting with the expert Daniela Pigosso and research within other platforms (will 
be described in part 9.2.) 
 -
During the classification, the reviewer should try to match the tool against the existing list, 
if not possible a discussion should be had to determine if a new type has to be added to the 
list.
Classification Criteria 5: User specialisation level
 -
Informing the user about the required level of specialisation to use the tool, and who the 
potential user should be; a generalist or a specialist.
 -
When evaluating the reviewer must estimate if the use of the tool could be done with a 
generalist background or whether a more formal training in a field is needed. The task can be 
difficult to undertake, and it is very dependent on the reviewer’s own perception and backg-
round, therefore the scoring is done only on a High/Low scale.
 
Classification Criteria 6: Application and acquisition cost
 -
Informing the user on what expense to expect if adopting the tool.
 -
Both the context and application of the tool is central in defining how the tool is perceived, 
so a high or low classification is seen in context to the scope and value of the tool. 
 -
Application and acquisition cost is generally set too low if the tool does not require investing 
in new hardware, software or draws significant amounts of internal resources.
 -
What states a significant amount will vary on the user, their size, their application and their 
perception of the value the tool can generate.
 -
The reviewer must do a rough estimation of whether the tool has a high or low cost.
Classification Criteria 7: Demanded time for use
 -
Informing the user on the time demand of the tool.
 -
Tools can be used as quick reference guides and others demand collection and analysis of vast 
amounts of data. Some can be scaled to fit the scope of a project. 
 -
The reviewer must do a rough estimation if the tools’ use demands are low as in only hours 
for a single person or more people, versus high as in days, weeks or months involving up to 
many stakeholders.
Classification Criteria 8: Current development level of  tool
 -
Describes how developed the prescribed tool is. 
 -
Some companies may be front runners and be willing to take risks or work associated with 
adopting new tools early in development. Other companies are more conservative in their 
approach and may only go for tried and tested tools. 
 -
The reviewer must estimate, based on the description in article, with the three options:
1. Theoretical if presented as new and only describes little or no testing.
2. Experimental if the tool has been tested on a few real cases or show application of an 
existing tool in a new context.
3. Consolidated if the tool has been well tested on real cases or is an updated version of 
an existing method or practice within the field. 
==PAGE:66==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 48
Classification Criteria 9: Input data
 -
Informing the user if the tool functions based on inputs of quantitative data (metrics) or 
qualitative data (knowledge) i.e. what is required from the user.
 -
The reviewer needs to identify the type of input data needed to use the tool: quantitative 
or qualitative data.
Classification Criteria 10: Output data
 -
Informing the user how the tool prescribes support in form of quantitative data (e.g. num-
bers to be analysed) or qualitative data e.g. suggestions to the user.
 -
Reviewer needs to identify the type of output data from the tool:  quantitative or qualitative 
data.
Classification Criteria 11: Information about the tool
 -
Describes whether the level of information found in the article about the tool is succinct or 
superficial. 
 -
Should the user expect to find all relevant information within the article or is the tool descri-
bed only superficially.
 -
The reviewer must evaluate if the description given about the tool is sufficient and well de-
scribed, or if it is superficial and the tool is only briefly or poorly described, or whether you 
need a good prior understanding of a specific area in order to understand it.
 3.1.2.4 Reﬂection about the systematisation 
The development of the classification criteria was based upon the existing understanding obtai-
ned during the RC and DS-1 stages. Since these stages were both review-based, no empirical study 
was made, as clarified in the methodology as part of the delimitation of this thesis. 
Where some of the criteria were relatively easy to perform on the tools, others were more com-
plex. 
An example are Criteria #9 Input and Criteria #10 Output that were relatively easy to identify, 
whereas it gets more difficult, and with the possibility of bias, to define Criteria 5 regarding User 
specialisation level. 
It was experienced how the authors, themselves as reviewers, tended to classify tools within their 
own professionalism, e.g. product development as 'low', potentially biased on how we could easily 
comprehend those compared to something belonging to other engineering skills or entirely dif-
ferent areas. The idea that whether it required a generalist or specialist helped in order to define 
it more consistently.
The low/high classifications were generally challenging, and it was proven that a more limited sca-
le is required. Furthermore, it was difficult to create a consistent "solution" of the criteria across 
all tools, among other things because these are also of such different nature. And especially when 
trying to relate it to a company context - what is low demanded time for use? And what is expensive? 
This depends not only on which company you deal with - it depends on the individual user's sub-
jectivity and experience. Therefore, they were used as a rough estimate of the tool, the authors 
==PAGE:67==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 49
being aware that there is room for improvement. This could either be through multiple reviews or 
validations, or in more qualitative values on the criteria. An example of this could be how Criteria 
5, about User specialisation level, could be ascribed with a specialist/generalist or something more 
significant, instead of the high/low option.
 3.1.3 Description of the ﬁnal backstage framework 
The backstage framework consists of a database of 49 classified tools that can help manufactu-
ring companies in the transition to CE in different ways and different stages. At this point, it is 
possible to search tools across 11 different filters and thereby specify relevant tools. This can be 
found in Appendix 14 and is illustrated in Picture 1. These criteria and their values are defined to 
classify the tools, so they can easily be identified both by a system (e.g. autogenerated) and by the 
users themselves. 
The tools are systematized in a spreadsheet. This was done for three reasons:
 -
First of all, to continue the structured and streamlined SLR process with built-in data capture.
 -
Secondly, in order to make the data available for filtering and thereby the identification. 
 -
Thirdly, to make it easy to maintain the data, update and build upon the database with new 
tools or make it possible to implement design changes in the future. 
3.1.3.1 Guide for how to maintain and update the backstage framework 
A full guide for how to maintain and update the backstage framework can be found in Appendix 
15. The main points are referred here:
Updating the backstage framework with new tools has to be done through the classification crite-
ria process. The auto-creation and exporting of merge data has to be done in Google Sheets (or 
Microsoft Excel).
1. In Google Sheets create an ID and insert article metadata
2. Classify the tools using the classification criteria defined in the SLR protocol (Appendix X) as 
a guide and fill in the corresponding columns. 
3. Download the “merge data” sheet as a .csv file and save it as toolr_mergedata to the folder /
Toolr/
4. Create new data merged tool pages using /Toolr/Tool_page.indd in Adobe InDesign “Data 
Merge” feature.

 3.2 Establish the goal for the PS stage 
The work carried out in DS-1, described in this section, has all together contributed to the devel-
opment of the support in the form of a backstage framework and an increased understanding 
of the research area. The result of the SLR was a final set of 47 included articles from where 49 
tools were extracted.

The result of the classification of the tools is a backstage framework that allows filtering across

This section presents the results from the Prescriptive Study which emp-
hasises the third phase: development of tool support. The chapter ends 
with a description of the final support tool concept.
  
As stated in Section 2.1, a support solution can be divided into form and 
medium. The form of the support tool was developed through the SLR 
and classification criteria process resulting in a backstage framework, a 
database of tools for Circular Economy, described in the previous secti-
on. The goal of this phase has been to develop the medium between the 
backstage framework and the users, an interface that can empower them 
in their identification of relevant tools based on their assessment results.
D E V E L O P M E N T  O F  T O O L  S U P P O R T
Chapter 4

In the development of the medium, the authors needed to open a "new" solution room after the 
form itself was developed. The PS stage has been characterized by a distinctive double-diamond 
mind-set, alternatingly diverging and converging against a solution, the starting point being the 
developed "back-stage framework" - how can a medium between this and the user be designed in 
order to increase usability of the support? And, what elements should the user be presented to?
Figure 24 presents the content of this section illustrated in the double-diamond model. This will 
be elaborated in the following sections.
Figure 24 - PS double diamond 
 4.1 Requirements Identiﬁcation
In this section, findings from literature, a research on existing platforms and identified stakeholder 
needs are synthesised into goals and functional requirements. The goals are interpretations by the 
authors of the vision for the support, whereas requirements have a functional and measurable 
nature. Both will be used to guide the conceptualisation of the medium.
 4.1.1 System users
The system users consist of two groups of users and are influenced by the delimiting related to 
the scope of the project. The descriptions of each group of users and the developed personas are 
based on the understanding developed through the previous research and based on the market 
research and interviews carried out in the stage, which will be presented in section 4.1.4. 
==PAGE:75==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 57
 4.1.1.1 Target users
The target users are employees within small, medium and large manufacturing companies since 
this is the primary scope of MATChE. These are the target users since they are the actors who 
will be directly involved in the process of developing and manufacturing. 
It is important to emphasize that this group in its entirety is very blackboxed, a simplification. 
Within this group, there are many different types of users/actors, jobs, qualifications, processes, 
etc. The specific interests and wishes of the individual actor for the development are not given or 
similar. Therefore, it can be said that the interests regarding Circular Economy are not assigned 
to the individual actor á priori (Olesen & Kroustrup 2007, p.72; apud Latour 1987). Even though, 
it has been assumed that since the users are engaging in CE transition on the MATChE platform 
prior to the context where the support should function, they are familiar with CE and engaged in 
the transition, but in various degrees and on different readiness levels. 
Small company
10-49 employees
Medium company
50-250 employees
Micro company 
<10 employees
Large company
>250 employees
Figure 25. Blackboxed target users 
It has therefore been a goal during this stage to explore and expand the understanding of this 
group to provide a more accurate insight into the various user types that can be found in this 
group. These users will therefore be further described through personas in section 4.1.4, when the 
understanding gained from interviews has been expanded.
 4.1.1.2 Secondary users 
The same applies to the secondary group that also encompasses stakeholders across sectors, jobs, 
goals and more. These secondary users can all be driven by an interest in circular economy within 
networking groups, consultancy, advocacy, research and academia in general. They are not the 
target group for MATChE nor the main focus, but relevant in the sense that the idea with MAT-
ChE is to expand the user involvement for the future. Thus, it is relevant to consider potential 
users outside the target users. It has therefore also been a goal, through this stage, to gain insight 
into how users from this group could be included in the support, which resulted in the persona 
“Consultant” presented in section 4.1.4.

Figure 26. An interpretation of the different networks around MATChE
 4.1.2 Exploring existing platforms  
Today, there are several online solutions available that try to gather methods and tools, which can 
inspire and aid businesses in the transition to work circularly. 
A review of different online websites has been conducted to identify existing solutions relatable 
to the areas of research: both as an objective analysis of content, number of tools and process 
focus of tools, but also the authors’ own subjective experience of the platform regarding usability. 
The selection of platforms was based on a ‘roll the snowball’ approach, partially based on the 
platforms that are available through MATChE and which had otherwise been encountered th-
rough the project. See an overview of the platforms in Figure 27. The Inclusion criteria were that 
they were publicly available, current and professionally prepared. Student projects like ‘Closing 
the Loop by Design’ and ‘CE toolkit’ were subsequently excluded. 
==PAGE:77==

The selected sites consist of combinations of content for these purposes:
Link repository 
 -
Enables search and selection of external tools or other type of resource that is available el-
sewhere on the internet.
Toolbox
 -
Contains a selection of tools specifically developed for the platform.
Guidance 
 -
The guidance is here defined as something that takes the user through steps and asks for in-
put data to select either a tool, strategy or present other relevant material. 
Case inspiration 
 -
Contains a selection of ‘best practices’ and relevant case studies striving to inspire and educa-
te the user. 
An overview of the sites can be seen in Appendix 16. Since the selected platforms have been 
introduced with a different focus and target, the comparison of them was sometimes like compa-
ring apples and oranges. However, the analysis underlined some important insights. 
Key findings can be seen on the opposite page.  
The market research underlined both the EU and Ellen MacArthur Foundation as some of the 
major initiators as both were engaged in several of the platforms. 
In addition, it also illustrated how the majority of these platforms are driven by stakeholders of 
various kinds; including stakeholders within the business, design, academic and legislative spheres. 
Typically, also organised with a stakeholder who is responsible for managing and communicating 
the platform. 
 4.1.3 Interviewing stakeholders
In addition to the market research, seven interviews were conducted as additional sources for the 
gathering of empirical knowledge supporting the development of requirements for the further 
development of the support. The purpose of these interviews was to gain a better understanding 
and develop insights on how stakeholders experience CE and then to ask more concretely about 
their workflows and current processes and how they are introduced today to new tools, methods, 
etc. The goal of the qualitative interviews was not to create a quantitative output but to increase 
the current understanding and get different points of view on the questions. 
 4.1.3.1 Clarifying prior hypotheses and planning execution of interviews
Based on the literature and conversations with stakeholders in the first part of the project, a set 
of hypotheses were formed around the subject of introducing new tools and working with trans-
formation in companies:

Overwhelming material: 
Many sites contain links to other sta-
keholders or toolboxes. There are no 
‘deeplinks’ – i.e. links directly to the pro-
posed tool nor an instruction/guide for 
it but just a link for another overview or 
platform, which makes the user experi-
ence superﬁ cial and chaotic.
Different origins and quality of 
material: 
Half of the platforms consist of material 
developed speciﬁ cally for this such as 
Circular Design Guide. The other half is 
what we call ‘link repository’, i.e. a tool 
for creating an overview and selecting 
resources of different kind elsewhere, 
such as SHIFT. The latter are therefore 
experienced as exploratory but also as 
less validated and, in terms of quality, 
difﬁ cult to comprehend.
Opaque selection process: 
Generally, there is a lack of transparen-
cy in how and why the tools are either 
developed or selected for the platform. 
The link between proposed tools and 
practical examples are for example 
non-existent. 
The originality of MATChE: 
MATChE is, based on this review, origi-
nal in terms of its online assessment tool 
that lets companies know their current 
readiness. Embedding Project has an 
assessment as well, but that is perfor-
med through physical meetings with the 
team behind the platform. 
Involvement of users: 
A way to evaluate tools and get fe-
edback on the material is to enable 
feedback from the users. This can also 
provide the MATChE team with date for 
either future research and empathises 
an open source thinking. The example 
is SHIFT that has a feature to review the 
tools both in a quantitative and qualita-
tive manner. 
Lack of graphical material: 
Many of the platforms, especially tho-
se referring to other pages, are very 
text-intensive and hard to get an over-
view of. On a website like ResCom, it 
works well to use graphics and icons 
to present the tools. The same layout 
is here used across the different tools 
which create the feeling of a thorough 
and consistent platform. 
Variation in the approach to select 
material: 
Several of the platforms have different 
ﬁ lterings and categorisations of the 
material that enable you to search for 
the same material based on different 
qualiﬁ cations. For example, a page like 
‘Embedding Project’ has both the ability 
to access material in a very broad sen-
se (Pathways) such as “Shape Identity” 
or through a more speciﬁ c practice e.g. 
“Share Stories”. This allows for more 
approaches, for example, depending 
on the user’s job, qualiﬁ cations, motiva-
tion etc.
Create cohesion between challenge 
and tool: 
ResCom (Circular Pathﬁ nder tool) and 
CirkVirk are both example of ‘virtual 
consultants’ where the user is guided 
through questions to understand their 
speciﬁ c opportunity. It works well to 
establish a connection between the 
challenge and the available material 
connected to that. This could be imple-
mented in the support by creating a re-
lation to the report companies generate 
by taking the assessment. 
Curriculums/Roadmaps: 
To cluster the different tools and put 
them in a sequential context, as seen 
on the SHIFT platform, works well and 
emphasizes the importance of thinking 
circularly throughout the value chain. 
However, this also requires the tools 
to be partly designed to complement 
each other.

To test the hypotheses and gather knowledge an interview guide was made where the articulation 
of the question both probed and challenged our initial understanding. This interview guide can 
be found in Appendix 17. New insights from interviews were added iteratively as questions in the 
guide for later interviews. 
 4.1.3.2 Establishing contact to stakeholders 
The selection of suitable candidates for the interviews was done aiming to reflect the potential 
users of MATChE, both the ones within the MATChE clusters (target users), networking groups 
and consultants (secondary users). 
Getting interviews with companies proved challenging for two main reasons; first, lack of time 
from the interviewee, “he is running around and doesn’t have time to talk” (said by a small chil-
dren’s clothing manufacturer), this type of response could already be used to support hypothesis 
number 5.  Second, dismissal to talk was either a polite version of the first or the lack of a con-
ceptual frame for the support tool function we described “I don’t think this is something for us” 
(said by a large building component manufacturer). This unfortunately lead to only a single full 
interview with a representative from the target group. Interviewing consultants that work daily 
with manufacturing companies proved more fruitful and could shed light on the types of challen-
ges facing SME’s and large enterprises. 
Due to the difficulties in getting a broad range of users (and mainly the lack of target users) it 
must be stated that the findings from these interviews may potentially be biased by the fact that 
most of the informants work as consultants and that almost all informants work within the en-
vironmental sector.
The interviews were conducted over telephone as semi-structured interviews to allow for new fin-
dings to be explored. Listed in Table 7 are the conducted interviews and their related appendices, 
which all contain quotes and a summary.  The participants have been anonymized using initials 
and a general description of the respective company. 
The interviews were analysed in two ways. The quotes were organised with an Affinity diagram to 
explore underlying themes, this will be elaborated in section 4.1.5. The answers were also summed 
up into short statements and added into the interview guide matrix (Sloth 2011) where each inter-
view has a column. The matrix made it possible to look for trends and deviations and to remove 
questions that resulted in irrelevant output.

The findings were used as input for the development stage. The whole interview matrix can be 
seen in Appendix 27, but the most important findings are summed and presented here: 
Question 3: Where do you see the value of  CE on a short and longer time scale?
 -
Finding: Understanding the value of CE is still in an early phase where most see value within 
marketing/branding or in environmental risk management/compliance. CE in relation to 
Business development is gaining attention.
 -
Implication: The support is more likely to succeed if it mediates the value people relates to 
CE.  
Question 4: Where within the organisation comes the initiative to work differently (with 
CE)?
 -
Finding: CE projects can be initiated from many places in a company but those that have 
CEO support are the ones with the most success. Only very large companies have a dedi-
cated CSR department. This supports hypotheses number 2 and is reflected in the personas 
described in section 4.1.4. 
 -
Implication: The medium should support the user with insights that can empower them in 
story-telling when trying to pitch the tool to decision makers e.g. top management.
Question 6: How do you generally plan and implement new initiatives/strategies/proces-
ses/development methods? 
 -
Finding: Consultants and network events play a central role in implementing new tools and 
methods
 -
Implication: We must include a consultant role in our personas.
Question 7: Are there ways/mediums that work better when trying to understand new 
==PAGE:82==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 64
possibilities within tools, methods, business models?
 -
Finding:  Consultants and network events are central in developing new insights. CE devel-
ops through knowledge sharing. At larger enterprises consultants may be internal experts or 
partnering companies that will act as consultants with specialized knowledge. 
 -
Implication: The medium should support multiple user types.
Question 8: What barriers are experienced when new initiatives are planned or to be im-
plemented?
 -
Finding: Time and skill are the most experienced barrier for implementing new initiatives. 
Time is possible to find, that is a question of willingness in allocating resources. Skill is harder 
to acquire or determine; may be through a consultant. 
 -
Implication: A tangible form of medium is important for users that are not in front of a 
computer all day. This way the support can emphasise their work practice. Furthermore, it 
can be used as a boundary object in more team-based settings, where the skills and resources 
available are more explicit. 
Question 9: Do you use (CE) tools or methods in projects, how and when were you intro-
duced to them?
 -
Finding: Network events, projects with partners and university collaborations are mentioned, 
along with knowledge coming in with new employees from schools or other businesses. No 
online toolkits were mentioned. The digital domain is not where people spend time learning; 
instead it happens when they set aside time to physically interact with others.
 -
Implication: The medium should support people’s interactions and learnings in the physical 
realm. 
Question 10: What would be important if  you/your costumers were to use the platform to 
evaluate if  a tool was relevant a problem?
 -
Finding: Make it easy to understand and use; less ‘academic language’, more graphics, simple 
layout. 
 -
Implication: The support medium should aim to be simple and easily understandable (to the 
extent that is possible with the current content of the backstage framework). 
Question 11: Do you see any day to day challenges (like getting stuck) in projects with 
new methods/tools?
 -
Finding: If the user cannot comprehend a system or a task, the chances are that the user will 
do something else. Regarding CE users are needed to be guided ‘hand-in-hand’ through the 
transition by consultants, tools, best practices or other guiding actors/artefacts. 
 -
Implication: The medium should be suggestive and provide guidance to the user. 
Question 12: Who are likely to benefit from the tool support?
 -
Finding: Companies who are already engaged in the transition, both SME’s and large enter-
prises. Consultants could use such a support to facilitate a conversation with the customer. 
 -
Implication: The users that will use this tool are already engaged in the transition and will 
==PAGE:83==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 65
probably be motivated but can have very different profiles and approaches to the support. 
Question 13: What does people do that is already successful in implementing initiatives?
 -
Finding: Consensus that people that are successful within CE are either born circular or have 
a clear business model with short ROI or where CE is the core of their business. 
 -
Implication:  The medium should help underline the way a tool creates value in a business
 4.1.4 Personas 
User personas were developed based on the research to represent a variety of user types, within 
the scope of MATChE that could potentially be users of the support. The creation of personas 
was used to aid the ideation process as a method to articulate the users’ needs, experiences and 
goals. Furthermore, it was important to design a medium that emphasises each of them since they 
were all potential users of the final support solution. Personas were based on insights gathered 
from interviews conducted and developed by clustering of insights in to four different personas: 
The CEO, The CSR Employee, The Production Manager and The Consultant, see Figure 28 on 
the next page. In the development of personas, the focus was on which motivators and obstacles 
they experience in their work. 
 4.1.4.1 Who is engaged in projects like MATChE?  
Generally, it depends on many different reasons, such as organisational structure, the company’s 
environmental profile, etc. Based on the interviews conducted, a number of statements have 
been made as to when the different personas can be involved in a project like MATChE and CE 
transition in general. 
The Business developer
The Business Developer can be engaged in all types of companies (small, medium and large). It 
depends on the specific company. It depends if Business Developer sees CE as an opportunity. 
The CSR Employee 
The CSR employee will mainly be engaged in very large companies, since CSR departments only 
exist there. In smaller companies, the persona can exist, but the title will change, and it will pro-
bably just be one person who has the CSR responsibility within the company. The CRS employee 
would probably be the initiator of such projects. The CSR department can vary widely in influen-
ce. In some companies, it is parked as the outermost part of the organisational structure, in other 
companies it is in direct contact with the top-level e.g. the business developer, which is the main 
barrier for whether or not the CRS employee succeeds with the initiatives. 
The R&D Employee
The R&D Employee will be engaged through either management or independently by searching

 4.1.4.2 Validation of the personas
The personas were subsequently validated and updated in collaboration with MM from a Regional 
Development company in a phone interview the 10th of May 2018, and again with SA and DS 
from a large textile service company at a Co-creation workshop held the 18th of May 2018. In 
the last session, it was stressed that both goals and obstacles can vary greatly from company to 
company, but that the personas basically assented consistent with their experiences. 
A final validation was done in the last phase of the project when testing the support. Here, test 
subjects (two potential users and one stakeholder) were asked to identify themselves and if they 
could recognize the other persona types from their work context. All three subjects validated the 
personas as being good and valid approximations.
 4.1.5 Afﬁnity Diagram 
The Affinity Diagram method (Beyer & Holtzblatt 1998) was used to explore and organize 
around 70 selected quotes from the interviews, each item was read out loud and discussed both 
as to its context and meaning (see Appendix 28). In the end, eight groups had formed with uni-
fying themes. The method helped uncover themes that had not been immediately visible from 
the interview alone and therefore provided knowledge on a new level. Some of the themes were 
expected as they had been central to the interviews, like ‘Identification of tools’, while others 
like ‘Competencies’ and ‘Knowledge exchange’ had not been articulated explicitly but appeared 
through the clustering.
The themes identified were: 
 -
Barriers
 -
Market Motivation
 -
Value Chain
 -
Identification of tools
 -
Knowledge Exchange
 -
Facilitating
 -
Competencies
 -
Value proposition

Each group were subsequently arranged and documented on a sheet of paper to be used as input 
in the development of a solution.

 4.2 Embody the backstage framework 
This chapter covers the development process towards a final support tool concept. It describes 
both the development of the medium, as in the embodiment of the support, but also how this 
and the backstage framework will function jointly. This will be developed on the basis of the iden-
tified requirements presented in the former chapter. The concept development is done in three 
iterations before the final concept for detailing is selected; an explorative idea generation iteration, 
a co-creating iteration and an evaluation iteration.
The focus of this section will be the communication of the tools. This is essential because the 
tools, as they exist today, in the form of articles, do not function as a boundary objects between 
academia and industry. There is a need for a translation that can make the tool comprehensible 
and tangible while, from a CE transition point of view, there is a need for companies to quickly 
see the value and potential of working circularly.
 4.2.1 The ﬁrst iteration – creative & systematic synthesis
The idea generation is exciting phase because this is where the results of the research is synthe-
sised and used to develop the intended impact. Prior to this section, the research has had the form 
of an external search based on literature and empirical data (Ulrich & Eppinger 2008). With the 
first iteration, the internal search process begins, where the authors’ own knowledge and creativity 
are used to generate concepts (Ulrich & Eppinger 2008). 
There are many methods to choose between which can be used at various stages of synthesis 
in support development to increase the solution space which is the aim of the idea generation 
phase. Nigel Cross (2008) divides synthesis methods into creative and systematic methods. Using 
both types in an iterative fashion ensures an explorative, but forward going process. This has 
been done by first identifying relevant features through brainstorming, prioritize them and then 
applying morphology chart (Cross 2008) as a method for generating concepts. See Figure 29 for 
an overview of the process. 
 4.2.1.1 What to develop? 
As requirement number 1 (see Table 8 in part 4.1.6) states, the support should function in conjun-
ction with the MATChE platform and assessment. A delimitation of the solution room has there-
fore been that the context and form of how to embody the backstage framework has been rather 
fixed. Furthermore, the content was also known as this was developed through the DS-1 stage. 
Instead, and in order to fit into MATChE, the development has been centred around how to 
implement the support in the MATChE Platform and develop this to makes sense with the asses-
==PAGE:89==

METHOD
DS-I
PS

OUTRO

Figure 29 - Overview of ideations process 
==PAGE:90==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 72
sment, and to do this in such a way that can enhance the usability of the backstage framework and 
translate the academic content into the network of MATChE users. The focus for this iteration 
has been on the presentation of the tools from the database and how the classification criteria 
should be included.
 4.2.1.2 Brainstorms 
A very open brainstorm was made based on the Affinity diagram with the purpose of generating 
all sorts of input to the grouped quotes. Then, the ideas were presented to discuss them together 
and remove possible overlapping ideas. All the ideas were clustered once again and labelled as 
either a feature or an insight (see Appendix 29).  
Here, feature means a directly implementable and functional element, where insight means impor-
tant elements of the context in which the medium should be designed.
Subsequent, groupings of the ideas for features resulted in the following seven areas:
User Experience
Ideas that focus on the overall experience that the support should create for the user
User Interface
Ideas that focus on how to graphically display content of the tool 
Business Model Elements
Ideas related to the elements of Business Model Canvas 
Physical Material
Ideas for developing physical and tangible material 
“Pick up where you left” 
Ideas that all aim to either autonomously save the user’s status or let the users record a history 
themselves 
“Ask for Help” 
Ideas for how users can seek help as a built-in feature
“To the point” 
Ideas for how the content of the tools can be presented in a succinct manner 
These groupings served as an inspiration and indicator of which overall elements were to be 
combined for a holistic concept.
 4.2.1.3 Scoring matrix
All of the ideas were subsequently ranked through a scoring matrix ranging in effort from easy 
==PAGE:91==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 73
to difficult in ‘implementation’ on the Y-axis as well as low and high ‘value’ on the X-axis (see 
Appendix 30). This proved to be a useful method for assessing each function on more than one 
parameter and balancing them in effort versus value. The effort of implementation was slightly 
diffused. With some features, it was meant to be the time to develop it, while others emphasise 
the realisation of this feature. For example, ”ask an expert” did not require much effort in pro-
gramming, but it would require MATChE to allocate resources to support this feature. This made 
it difficult to implement. Following ideas were prioritised:
 -
Make tool tangible - e.g. by print or pdf 
 -
Mark interest in/Save tool 
 -
Graphic representation of tool information
 -
”Personal” note feature – make comments possible 
 -
Value tags – underline bottom-line value of the tool
 -
Suggest tools in the assessment report
 -
Glossary list
 -
Guide for how to select tools 
 -
A function that enables the comparison of the tools 
 4.2.1.4 Overall function of support 
Two ideas of the overall function of the support were also ranked in the matrix: an integrated 
version and a stand-alone version. 
The integrated version means that the support tool is fully integrated in the my.MATChE Plat-
form and the user will be provided with tool suggestions e.g. as part of the results or assessment 
report. The tools will be auto generated exactly like the report are today so that they would match 
the readiness level of the company. This way the support would have a suggestive approach. 
The stand-alone version means that the support tool is independent of MATChE and is its own 
platform, as the ones explored in part 4.1.2. The users use filters to generate tools, either based on 
an assessment or not. This way the support would have an explorative approach. 
The integrated version scored (1), where the stand-alone version scored (-2). This is due to how 
the integrated version provides value to the user because it creates a direct link between their 
current readiness and relevant tools. It builds upon the MATChE thinking: that one needs to 
assess the current level in order to transition. However, the stand-alone version was not desele-
cted, despite the rating, as it lives up to the demanded requirement (see Table 8, part 4.1.6) that 
the support should function without a prior assessment and because the authors believe that the 
exploratory approach can also have value. In addition, the stand-alone version meets the desire to 
create visibility and transparency in research within Circular Tools. Therefore, work was continued 
to see how these two main functions could be united in one concept.
==PAGE:92==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 74
 4.2.1.5 Morphology Chart 
The best ranked ideas as well as the classification criteria that classify the tools were used for a 
morphology chart (see Appendix 31 or Figure 29). These can be seen in the vertical column ‘Ele-
ments’ divided into respectively ‘UI features’ and ‘Tool information’. The aim was to generate a 
range of alternative concepts for the presentation of the tool to increase the understanding of 
how the combination of information and features could enhance the identification of the tool’s 
purpose and value. The ‘solutions’ row are varying ways to present the elements; graphical, text, 
both, other ways or not there. Four different concepts were configured by combining elements 
and solutions from the morphology, see Figure 30. These concepts represent different possible 
configurations of layouts for the tool page and were generated in slightly exaggerated editions, i.e. 
going on a scale from ‘information heavy’ to ‘information light’ and again going from ‘graphically 
heavy’ to a more ‘text heavy’ layout.

 4.2.2 The second iteration – co-creation
The authors planned a workshop, including a co-creation exercise with a large textile service com-
pany for the 18th of May 2018. The company rent, wash and deliver service on workwear, linen, 
mats and towel rolls for hospitals, hotels and some of Denmark’s largest companies. This also 
means that their primary business is service oriented, and therefore does not apply to the main 
target group of MATChE. But, in light of their high material use and work practices they were 
seen highly relevant as a stakeholder. 
Participating in the workshop was SA and DS from the CSR department and the workshop was 
held in Danish. The company works extensively with circular initiatives both within the company 
and through their partnerships, and SD and DS explained how they, in the CSR department, were 
in close dialogue with management because their CEO ”sees circular economy and sustainability 
as the future”. 
The decision to arrange a workshop with a company was made because they, based on a readiness 
assessment and otherwise shaped by their daily experience, could provide constructive feedback 
and relevant insights to improve the communication of the tools. A workshop plan can be seen 
in Appendix 32. 
The workshop was organized for the following purposes:
 -
Generate a sample of tools to be used in the prototype and for testing based on MATChE 
assessment results and prioritisation of focus area 
 -
Generate further insights and inputs than the ones already developed
 -
Validate the personas
 -
Co-create and develop concepts of how to present tool information in order to help compa-
nies in the identification of relevance  
 4.2.2.1 Sample for prototype 
The prototype will ultimately illustrate how the tools could be presented in a way that allows users 
to identify the relevance and value of this particular tool. This translation of the article strives to 
ensure that users can understand and use the tool. In order to develop this qualitative medium of 
the tools, a delimitation has been to work more focused with a selection of tools: a sample.

would take a readiness assessment and subsequently prioritise the dimensions based on a priority

According to the outcome, their readiness within the specific dimension would define which tools 
were further developed for the prototype of the support.

 4.2.2.2 Stage discussion exercises 
The idea for them to go through the readiness assessment was, in addition to generating a sample, 
that they should be primed to co-create an information page about a tool.
They received a brief introduction to the project about the development of the backstage fra-
mework. Subsequently, they received an introduction about the motivation in relation to increase 
the usability of the database and the aim of translating the tool from the scientific article to in-
crease the user’s ability to identify relevance.
The workshop consisted of two exercises before the actual co-creation exercise; a quick brain-
storm that had the potential to reveal new features and a validation of personas. The result of this 
could also be part of the co-creation exercise afterwards. 
The first exercise resulted in features/tool information that were already part of our input, but 
also new ones such as:
 -
Return on Investment (RoI)
 -
What is new within the tools compared to existing tools?
 -
Links to relating articles 
Picture 6 & 7 – Brainstorm  
Instead of the actual purpose of the persona exercise, which was to make them articulate chal-
lenges each persona will have and that they could later use as inspiration for co-creating, it facili-
tated a good talk and gave them the opportunity to validate the personas. The feedback was very 
positive and resulted in some small changes. An important insight was that the challenges of the 
personas would vary widely from company to company, depending on the company’s overall goal 
and strategy in relation to CE. They did not find that they were far from idea to action in the CSR 
==PAGE:97==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 79
department, but they also believed that was because CE was highly prioritized in their company. 
 4.2.2.3 Co-creation exercises 
The co-creation exercise consisted of putting together a tool page of the information and fea-
tures we brought, as well as the ones they had developed in the brainstorm exercise. Afterwards 
they had to prioritise the most important information about the tool in order for the tools to be 
presented briefly in the readiness assessment, for example.
Picture 8 & 9 – Developing tool pages 
It was exciting to observe how different an approach the participants took to the development 
of the tool page. They were very different in what information they wanted and in which order. 
Overall insights were:
 -
Some of the classifications that defines to either high/low are difficult to understand. Instead 
they can act more as “showstoppers” e.g. if the user sees that the demanded time or the acqu-
isition cost is high.
 -
Too much information can be overwhelming. Instead it should be allowed for users to seek 
the information if they are interested, e.g. by making the information clickable (if on a web-
site). 
 -
Icons do not speak for themselves (in this context). For example, it is hard to remember the 
MATChE dimensions. This is why you need text, and not just icons for it to function pro-
perly.
 -
The “fast-read” aggregated text ‘about this tool’ is most important. The more prescriptive, 
==PAGE:98==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 80
for example by writing the application steps, the better.
The co-creation exercise resulted in two concepts; a A4 tool page (see Picture 9) that should 
enable the user to identify relevance and prioritized information for a ‘tool slip-page’, a short 
introduction to the tool, e.g. to be used in the assessment. Further findings and pictures from the 
workshop can be found in Appendix 33. 
 4.2.3 The third iteration – Developing prototype 
The subsequent iteration consisted of collecting and creating a combined concept for a prototype 
of the support based on the prior development work throughout the project. And also by using 
methods such as User Journeys (with inspiration from the consumer decision journey (Court et 
al. 2009)) and Service Blueprint (Shostack 1984) in order to illustrate how the users interact with 
the support and how the frontstage medium interacts with the backstage framework. 
Focus has been on the overall user journey and how the support tool in form of backstage fra-
mework and tool pages can be designed into the MATChE context to get the best possible results 
and continue the user’s journey from the readiness assessment towards accelerating their transiti-
on. In order to fulfil both a suggestive and explorative approach described in section 4.2.1.4, the 
support is developed as an independent platform that works in conjunction with the MATChE 
platform. Tools will be generated based on user’s readiness assessment, but the user will also be 
able to filter tools themselves
This combination of approaches has been chosen because:
 -
The authors regard the readiness assessment as being extremely important in creating a foun-
dation for identifying relevant tools 
 -
It is important to build upon the user journey from MATChE and create the next step for 
the companies to take
 -
It allows more users to access the platform, such as secondary users who are not connected 
to MATChE. This availability of the material outside of my.MATChE would also support the 
research-related perspective in increasing the overview of current research.
 -
It enables users to generate tools that do not appear due to their readiness assessment. It is 
believed that people feel most confident in their decisions when they can compare and eva-
luate them (Roller 2010).
 -
Lastly, the authors believe that more and more companies will be proactive in circular soluti-
ons and will seek solutions themselves or through consultants who can use the support as a 
facilitating tool.
The aims in the iteration was to explore:
 -
How can the tool pages be designed to enable expansion of the database?
 -
How can the support be integrated as part of the my.MATChE assesment? 
 -
How can the support be designed to function without prior assessment? 
==PAGE:99==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 81
- 
How will the frontstage interface receive input from the backstage framework?

link was to design these tool pages, so they could also easily be updated. The backstage framework 
is structured with data in a way that allows a similar, structured way of handling and extracting 
data as tool pages both for pdf/print and for digital distribution, namely by Data Merging. Data

pages for each of the 49 tools. See Appendix 34 for an elaboration of this process. Data merging 
also helped generate elements for the prototype in an effective manner.

REMANUFACTURING SIMULATION MODEL
Tool type
Simulation
Simulation framework for predicting the performance of 
remanufacturing systems operating under various production 
control policies within a digital environment, before the 
implementation in the real system. A generic and reconfigurable 
simulation model.
The article was authored by Gaspari L., Colucci L., Butzer S., Colledani M., Steinhilper R. 
Published in Journal of Remanufacturing Volume: 7 in 2017. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-84977260191&doi=10.1515%2fcer-2016-0013&partner-
ID=40&md5=27e3fb6680b418971e4c00aa49c4bd7b
Described in the 2017 article: 
Modularization in material flow simulation for managing production releases in reman-
ufacturing
Abstract from article
Remanufacturing is recognized as a major circular economy option to recover and upgrade func-
tions from post-use products. However, the inefficiencies associated with operations, mainly due 
to the uncertainty and variability of material flows and product conditions, undermine the growth 
of remanufacturing. With the objective of supporting the design and management of more pro-
ficient and robust remanufacturing processes, this paper proposes a generic and reconfigurable 
simulation model of remanufacturing systems. The developed model relies upon a modular 
framework that enables the user to handle multiple process settings and production control poli-
cies, among which token-based policies. Customizable to the characteristics of the process under 
analysis, this model can support logistics performance evaluation of different production control 
policies, thus enabling the selection of the optimal policy in specific business contexts. The pro-
posed model is applied to a real remanufacturing environment in order to validate and demon-
strate its applicability and benefits in the industrial settings. ¬© 2017, Springer Science+Business 
Media B.V.

 4.2.3.2 Exploring integration through User journeys and Service Blueprints
In order to establish an understanding of the user journey to build upon and the context where the 
support should function, the present journey of taking a readiness assessment was explored in Figure 
31. As of today, the results can either be read on the website by clicking the dimensions or the user 
can download a PDF version. These two approaches will result in two different user experiences 
which the support should function in. This was further explored through a Service Blueprint, which 
can be seen in Figure 32 on the next page, in order to understand the different levels of interaction. 
Figure 31 – The current user journey when taking a my.MATChE assessment
From the assessment and the last step of the user journey, it is assumed that the users will now 
have acquired a general knowledge about CE transition and the MATChE elements used to com-
municate this, i.e. the dimensions, sub-dimensions and readiness levels. This will enable their pos-
sibility to link that knowledge to the tools and thereby be able to identify relevance. This will, of 
course, only be the case if the user has taken a readiness assessment. It is not possible to define 
the starting point for users who have not taken this assessment.
The integration then took place in order to allow two user journeys:
A primary user journey: This journey is an extension of the present my.MATChE user journey. 
The user will be within the target group and has taken the readiness assessment. Suggested tools 
will be added to the user’s result page. 
A secondary user journey: “Autonomous” and proactive users, mainly within the secondary user 
group, that independently seeks the database. This means that they have not taken a prior asses-
sment.

Figure 32 – The current Service Blueprint for my.MATChE assessment

adding to the existing one seen in Figure 31 and Figure 32. This was gathered to a final Service 
Blueprint in Figure 33 illustrating the support through user actions, front-stage interactions and 
support processes, which are, among others, how the medium of the prototype functions with

 4.2.3.3 Brainstorm of name 
A quick brainstorm around the name of the support was held. The name should meet the follow-
ing three requirements: Work with the name ‘MATChE’, Indicating the purpose of the platform 
and work to be used in a sentence, so it is easy to talk about. The final support solution was given

 4.2.3.4 Embodiment of Design - Prototyping the Toolr website 
In order to embody the support, a prototype of the Toolr website was created. This was done by 
first sketching up a number of wireframes of the integration on the my.MATChE platform and 
the intended pages on the individual Toolr website. Subsequently, these wireframes were digita-
lized using software Adobe XD and interactions were added to some of the buttons in order to 
navigate the website. 
The purpose of the prototype was twofold: To embody and propose a direction for a holistic 
support design and to act as a boundary object to test with users in the following stage in order to 
get feedback and evaluate the support.

used and possible implications within the support solution (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009, p.302).

Different use cases exist; self-assessment or guided assessment with a consultant.

Target users are manufacturing companies and all of the employees within. Secondary users are researchers 
and consultants. The level of knowledge and familiarity about CE will vary a lot, but they will be introduced 
and educated about CE through the ﬁrst steps of MATChE. The support is then intended to provide knowledge

prior research done by the MATChE team. 
Maintenance 
The data stored in the support does not need to be updated. Maintenance involved to keep the support rele-

 4.4 Description of the ﬁnal support tool 
.concept 
The Toolr website enables users to explore and identify tools for circular economy, either guided 
through MATChE upon assessment results or through the filter function. 
The final prototype consists of the following two main elements: 
 -
An early proposal for the website Toolr that, partly integrated with the MATChE platform, 
can suggest tools. Either based on the user’s readiness assessment as part of my.MATChE 
readiness profile or through how the user, on their own, can use different filters to search the 
database.
 -
Data merged tool pages that as a translation on the scientific article should increase compa-
nies’ ability to identify the relevance of the specific tool. These are part of the Toolr website.

In this stage, the goal has been to do an initial evaluation of the support 
(Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009, p.195) both in relation to the design of 
form i.e. the backstage database as well as the design of the medium 
i.e. the interface the user is presented with trough the prototype. These 
two parts are described in previous sections, respectively, Chapters 3 & 4. 
Due to the scope and time limits of the project, the support had only 
been developed to a prototype level, but an initial evaluation could give 
indications of its applicability, usability and usefulness. In part 2.5 Suc-
cess criteria, two measurable success criteria had been established: Is it 
useful? and Is it usable? which were the two questions sought answered 
in this section.

future iterations were exposed.

 5.1 Evaluation plan
The support design and prototype has been tested to evaluate the proposed support solution with 
both target users, secondary users and the team behind MATChE with the intention of addres-
sing the relevance and appropriateness of the system. As described in section 4.1.1, the concept 
involves different groups of users: the target group, which contains users within manufacturing 
companies, and secondary users which mainly consists of consultants and MATChE being the 
platform facilitator.
The success and desired impact of the support is thought to require validation of all user groups. 
Nevertheless, the most important users are believed to be the users within manufacturing compa-
nies, since they are the target group of MATChE with practical needs to be solved. 
 5.1.1 Recruitment
The recruitment process of test participants has been motivated by getting both existing and 
potential users of MATChE. Recruitment of the target group has been difficult throughout the 
project and thus also for user tests. It has proved much easier to establish relationships with con-
sultants and various interest groups. Listed in Table 14 is an overview of the final test participants 
and which tests they participated in. The participants have been anonymized using initials and a 
general description of the respective company.

 5.2 Test of tool relevance 
This test was based on the backstage database and thus the systematisation of tools in relation to 
whether the filtering allows for relevant tools to be extracted. The objective of the first test was 
therefore to validate the relevance of the content of the support i.e. the tools, based on Success 
Criterion 1 stated in section 2.5:
Success Criterion 1: Ability to identify tools
 -
Measurable success criteria: Is it useful? 
A product that is useful, allows the user to accomplish a task or objective. In this case, that is if the 
support tool allows the user to identify relevant tools in order to test both the content in general 
and the filtering. 
 5.2.1 Test setup
The final support tool is based on a systematic literature analysis with the result of a backstage 
framework that contains classified tools and enables filtering to search relevant material or to ge-
nerate tool suggestions based on the readiness assessment. This systematisation is based on the 
authors’ partially subjective approach to a broad spectrum of knowledge and therefore it has been 
relevant to conduct tests that could validate the final content. 
The test has been based on the tool sample (see Table 9) that came into existence after co-creation 
with the textile service company, i.e. tools based on their readiness assessment within the “Orga-
nisation” dimension; a total of 7 tools. In addition, the test included a fake tool, thus not relevant 
to this dimension. This was done as a validation of their ability to discover tools that should not 
be relevant at all. 
The test was carried out with two participants, SA and DS, from the textile service company and 
LP from the MATChE team, the former correctly primed after carrying out the assessment. The 
test was carried out in the form of presentation of the articles on the data-edited tools and a sub-
sequent compilation on a scale of 1 (no relevance) to 5 (high relevance) on each tool. It was set 
up as a questionnaire using Google Analytics (see Appendix 37) sent by email to SA and DS and 
conducted during a meeting with LP. The questions were divided into two; whether they saw it 
as relevant in the context of the sub-dimension and if it was relevant for them as ‘users’. LP was 
meant to represent the MATChE users. 
 5.2.2 Usefulness of tools 
Scorings from the two tests were analysed in Appendix 38. In both tests, the test subjects were 
==PAGE:118==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 100
able to identify the false tool as of low relevance, scoring 3 for the textile service company and 1 
for LP, which was a positive indication of them being valid informants. 
Removing the false tool from the data, the textile service company’s average relevance score for 
the sub-dimensions were 4,13 and LP scored 4,29. This validates the idea that the tools were rele-
vantly filtered for the sub-dimensions. 
Regarding the relevance for the textile service company as users, it was expected that the tools 
would score lower as not all tools are expected to be relevant for all companies. The average score 
was 3,57, an average generated upon both some high and some low scores. LP was expected to 
score higher representing the MATChE users and did so with a 4,17 average.
Overall the validation proved positive; both in relation to the tools being relevant and that the tool 
pages could convey sufficient information for users to evaluate the relevance, overall the support 
solution being useful. 
Picture 16 - Testing of tool relevance with tool pages
 5.2.3 Reﬂection on tool relevance tests
Upon reflecting on the validation process, it was found that the prototype sample inadvertently 
may have been based on the hardest dimension for the test subjects to evaluate. ‘Organisation’ is 
a dimension that in some sense flows across dimensions of a company. Furthermore, evaluating 
if a tool is relevant in a sub-dimension called ‘Processes & Tools’ can become a meta experience, 
and ‘Business Case’ is at the core of CE and can to a certain extend be said to be in all tools. 
Other dimensions are more succinct in the domain focus and would probably have been easier 
to evaluate. 
==PAGE:119==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 101
 5.3 Usability test 
To evaluate the prototype of the Toolr website and its integration with the MATChE platform, 
usability tests were conducted. Usability goes beyond the solution being useful and examines the 
way it enables ease of use and learnability of the solution. The test aimed to evaluate usability in 
relation to the second Success Criteria stated in section 2.5:
Success Criterion 2: Ability to comprehend value of  tools
 -
Measurable success criteria: Is it usable? 
Furthermore, to answer the question:
How do the users perceive the value of  the support and the context of  use?  
The method used has been the Think-aloud protocol introduced by Clayton Lewis in 1982. This 
involves the participants thinking aloud when they perform specified tasks that are crucial for 
the function of the medium. Participants are instructed to do so, by telling them to say whatever 
comes into their mind while performing the given tasks e.g. what they do, look for, think and feel 
while doing it. 
The testing followed an interview guide (see Appendix 39) designed so that one of the authors 
was given the role of interviewer and facilitated the test, whereas the other was observer and took 
notes to gather concurrent data. The test consisted of a short introduction about the project and 
a pitch about Toolr, a short conversation about the participants’ job, an optional MATChE readi-
ness assessment (if they were not familiar with MATChE) in order to prime them to the context 
and afterwards the instructed test of the Toolr prototype. During the usability test the participants 
were given several tasks to perform in the prototype, e.g. “You find a tool interesting. Try to look for 
more information about this tool and tell us how you evaluate the relevance from the given information”. See Ap-
pendix 39 for a list of the different tasks. 
The test was furthermore designed with an evaluation in plenum afterwards as well as a usabi-
lity questionnaire (see Appendix 40) allowing for retrospective data that could potentially reveal 
additional or more reflective insights. The session was audio recorded with permission from the 
individual participants. 
 5.3.1 Observations from usability test
From the tasks given in the prototype session and the following evaluation questions, a very mixed 
feedback was given (see Appendix 41). The usability questionnaire resulted in the feedback seen 
in Table 15.
