Table 15 – Feedback from usability questionnaire
In general, the prototype and idea behind the support solution was positively received. It was both 
mentioned as a tool one could see being used by consultants and another participant said it had 
the potential to be used by “highly specialists”, which of course is of different character, but sums 
up that it is usable in terms of its overall function and context. 
However, most of the comments related to the usability test could be seen as negative but were 
the users’ suggestions for how the design could be improved and given as recommendations th-
roughout the tests. The observations from the tests pointed in many different directions, but were 
afterwards clustered to the following findings related to improvement, divided into whether they 
are ‘short term’ or ‘long term’ implementable:
 5.3.1.1 Short-term improvements 
These are the main findings that are either already implemented or relatively easy to change for an 
improvement of the usability and applicability of the solution.
Support workflows
The idea behind having a platform that you can leave and still have your work saved was well re-
ceived, as it is seen with both my.MATChE and my.Toolr. This way it makes the users able to leave 
the computer but having the tools that they found interesting, saved. The same was experienced 
with the tangible outputs in the form of pdf versions of tool pages and recommendation reports. 
This allows users to print the material, take it to meetings or have it on their desk as a reminder. 
==PAGE:121==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 103
Picture 17 - Test participants exploring Toolr prototype
==PAGE:122==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 104
More narrative approach
This finding was in relation to the translation of the tools and proved the finding made throug-
hout the project that the language used in academia is much different from the language spoken 
in the industry. 
The users had trouble understanding the headlines like “Application level”, “Dimension relevan-
ce”, and “Value”. Having a more narrative/descriptive communication like “This tool can help 
you in” or “Financially this tool will help your company with” could improve the support. 
Layout 
Both Toolr and MATChE were reported to be too text heavy and users were observed not ac-
tually reading. There seems to be an information overload especially on the MATChE results 
pages, now also including the tool suggestions. In addition, both platforms are not meant to be 
used in everyday life on a longer basis, which is why the learnability is low. Therefore, it should be 
designed as a simple website with the possibility of few simple learnable elements due to the fact 
that users do not experience an increasing learning curve, but rather a more abrupt learning that 
occurs when they use the platform again. This could be implemented by a stronger coherence 
between how MATChE explains and uses graphics, than it is seen now in the prototype. Regar-
ding simplicity, all things that ‘disturbs’ should be removed. This was for example the case with 
the images of the tool, where people tried to read the information on them and became confused, 
not seeing them as just thumbnail images.
Database Filters
The database filter page received good feedback. It proved to have good learnability mainly 
because it looks like something they knew from other websites. The improvement could be a 
more sophisticated way to structure and design the filters, which is a short-term improvement. In 
the longer run, it should be investigated if other filters should be included (or the ones that were 
excluded). This would take more empirical data to conclude on. 
 5.3.1.2 Long-term improvements 
These solutions require some more work in one or two earlier stages and are therefore estimated 
to require more time but will also create a bigger impact and improvement of the support being 
applicable. For example, solutions that require a more in-depth analysis of the tools and articles 
behind them or new classification criteria to support new information on the tool pages.
Narrow down the options 
”Be bolder! Dare to suggest just one tool” was said by a participant. The assumption made about 
how the users want to know what they are choosing from seemed to be proven wrong since a lot 
of the users were intimidated about the selection of tools suggested in the integrated MATChE 
part. This could, of course, also be due to the design of how they are suggested, which definitely 
could be more sophisticated. But it is also a question of user type - some want a selection and to 
make the decision themselves, some want the system to make a decision for them. Whether this is 
easy or takes more time to implement is hard to define, but small improvements on how to display 
==PAGE:123==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 105
the suggested tools could already create a positive impact. The system can, over time, also learn 
to be more sophisticated in the suggestions, e.g. through the ‘save this tool’ feature, that could 
highlight ‘popular’ tools. This would require some more input-based features, like a rating-system, 
to the website in order to function properly though.
Tool information and classifications  
A general request for more information on the tool pages was explored in all of the tests. 
Here, there is a balance between creating lots of analysed material on the tools and allowing for 
a manageable classification load which, also, must be taken into account. More studies on what 
information is relevant in order for identification must be done with the users of MATChE in 
relation to create a more meaningful identification. 
For the latter, a source of error here was that more participants were struggling to understand the 
boundaries of the tool pages as being able to identify the relevance and thus did not facilitate the 
companies learning to use the tool. 
 5.3.1.3 Usability of the support
It was found that the usability of the support in relation to the overall idea was well received as 
was the idea of having a tool suggested matching one’s readiness level. Generally, the prototype 
of the support scored higher on utility and scope, but low on simplicity and coherence. This was 
to be expected, since the prototype of the support was made on a very initial basis. 
 5.3.2 Reﬂection on usability tests
After the two first usability tests conducted, it became clear to the authors that they had been too 
eager to test the functionality of the support i.e. the usability, where the real value in the test was 
more in the applicability of the support. These tests had been conducted in the ‘original spirit’ of 
usability tests for interfaces, where the user has to solve the tasks themselves without any priming 
or help given by the instructor. The intention with this is that the real value is sometimes hidden 
in the struggle of solving tasks. The result of this was that the two first test subjects had spent 
more energy on the navigation element of the solution. It would actually have been more intere-
sting to have given them more help or designed the test using another method, so their focus had 
been more on how they perceived the value within the elements of the prototype rather than the 
functionality of it being a navigable website. 
These learnings were implemented in the last tests where the instructor facilitated with more help 
during the tasks. This proved really positive in order to shed more light on the value within the 
support and less on the functionality of the prototype. Furthermore, the effect of the facilitation 
was apparent in the behaviour of the test persons and resulted in less frustration and eventually 
also reflected in a more positive mind set about the application of the solution. 
==PAGE:124==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 106
Also, a source of error in the first two tests was that the introduction to the test was not com-
municated properly and the participants did not understand the delimitation of the project. For 
example, one participant was most interested in getting case stories, which was not part of the 
material in the backstage framework. But since the final set of included articles were based on the 
inclusion criteria that the tool presented at least was validated through a case; this finding could 
indicate that the cases presented in the articles should have been highlighted in the narrative of 
the tools.
Another reflection deals with the degree of detail in the prototype. It could also have proved more 
useful to have made a more low-fi version of Toolr, for example in paper, and thus removed the 
focus from the functionality. This could potentially also have been less intimidating on the parti-
cipants that sometimes seem to believe that they failed in navigating a fully functional prototype. 
==PAGE:125==
==PAGE:126==
==PAGE:127==
page 109
The work carried out in this project has resulted in the design of a support 
tool compiled of a backstage framework containing tools for circular eco-
nomy presented in the website Toolr. 
The Toolr website and tool information pages enables users to identify tools 
related to their company and level of readiness in relation to transition to 
Circular Economy. The identification can either happen through suggestions 
in the company’s results from the MATChE readiness assessment or through 
the users on their own using the Toolr website to search tools through diffe-
rent filters.  The support tool is based on an explorative research clarification, 
a systematic literature review of 453 published studies, insights from users 
and the outputs from creative and systematic design processes.
In this section the results of the thesis are discussed in relation to the aims of 
this thesis and also in relation to reflections created throughout the process. 
This is followed by an overall conclusion thesis and limitations of the thesis. 
Afterwards the authors reflect on suggestions for future work, that follow 
from these findings
D I S C U S S I O N  &  C O N C L U S I O N
Chapter 6
==PAGE:128==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 110
 6.1 Discussion
This chapter will discuss the results of the thesis, evaluating the concept against the overall aim 
of the thesis – while incorporating results from the tests and evaluation. 
 6.1.1  System boundaries
Based on the Research Clarification phase, which included a literature review and project brief 
given by the MATChE research team, a need for CE initiatives that can support the implemental 
stages on the micro level was identified. But at the same time, it was identified that without an 
overview of current knowledge, it is not possible to determine specific research gaps. From this 
the overall issue of interest for the thesis was defined to circular economy transition, the activi-
ty being the development of a support tool that can identify relevant CE tools and the area of 
application being manufacturing companies. Throughout the project, both in regarding literature 
and empirical data, the otherwise delimited problem space was rather complex. The development 
arena around CE is difficult to illustrate and findings from the literature pointed towards how this 
is due to the diversity of elements in socio-technical systems (Jackson et al. 2014; apud Markard 
et al. 2012). Furthermore, the target group for MATChE, and therefore also for this thesis, being 
manufacturing companies also enlarges the problem space because the interests, motivations, or-
ganisational structures, qualifications etc. within these companies are very different. 
However, a system and holistic thinking is required in order for changes at the individual levels 
(Ghisellini et al. 2016; Murray et al. 2017; Franco 2017), and this thesis adopted the system thin-
king that the MATChE platform is built upon: that CE encompasses many dimensions and that 
in spite of differences and stance in relation to CE, there will still be similarities between the 
companies. The development through the thesis has therefore focused on how the support tool 
could encompass the system thinking of CE, the diversity of both manufacturing companies and 
specific user approaches in how the support tool generates and presents tool. The evaluation from 
the PS and DS-2 stages indicated that companies are having difficulties in relating to the system 
thinking: that all dimensions, to a greater or lesser extent, ultimately are relevant to their transition. 
Much reluctance was shown as soon as something that is not in the specific scope of the company 
is presented. This was both experienced during interviews, but also in the workshop where the 
participating company, being mainly a service provider, eventually fell out of MATChE’s scope. 
At first this contributed to a changed and more nonchalant attitude towards the readiness assess-
ment, which changed when they experienced many of the questions being relevant. One finding 
was therefore to develop a way to exclude ‘noise’, all the irrelevant tools, and to refine tools sug-
gestions.
In an attempt to empathise this issue, the authors developed a way to increase the customisation 
==PAGE:129==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 111
of the user experience with best-fit tools: tools that have been suggested the most on the basis of 
the whole readiness assessment. This received postive feedback in DS-2, but further reflections 
about how this is presented is required.
 6.1.2  Impact of the support tool
A finding from the literature review, stated in Chapter 1, was that more research is needed to 
support the bottom-up level, the manufacturing industries (Franco 2017; Linder et al. 2017; Elia 
et al. 2017; Lieder et al. 2017). The SLR in this thesis demonstrated that the research exists, but a 
translation and systematisation of the academic material is needed in order for companies to take 
it into use. The authors believe that the translation of the material is of most importance. The 
way the found tools are presented in academic articles is not applicable in a company context. 
This translation has now been brought closer by creating an overview and systematising the tools 
in the backstage framework, developed in DS-1. Subsequently, the translation was enhanced by 
bringing it into context in the form of a support tool, developed in PS, with the integration in 
MATChE as the main value.
This was evident in the final stage, DS-2, where tests showed that participants could identify the 
relevance of the tools from the tool pages and they furthermore proved to actually be relevant for 
the scope. In addition, the overall concept also received positive feedback. The support solution 
can therefore be said to have an impact in relation to that knowledge that was previously difficult 
to access has become available. However, the tests also substantiated that the translation can be 
improved in order for the companies to comprehend the value of the tools. 
 6.1.3  Approach 
The thesis has been a collaboration with the research team behind the MATChE platform, with 
the purpose of developing a support solution that could be integrated in MATChE. The thesis 
has therefore been based on research and assumptions prior to the thesis and the initial work, 
ideas and visions behind MATChE. Accordingly, the outcome of the thesis has been greatly influ-
enced by the existing platform, in order to be able to integrate the support in a feasible manner. 
It has been attempted to consciously create a critical approach to MATChE and the underlying 
assumptions that the scope of this thesis has, partially, derived from. However, the final support 
concept remains very influenced by the MATChE approach to CE. It is therefore possible, that 
if the project had not been intended to build on to the readiness assessment, the final concept 
proposal could potentially have looked different. This could all go back to the design of the SLR 
protocol in relation to the design of the search strings. Nevertheless, and as it was explored, se-
veral of the MATChE elements, such as dimensions, relates and corresponds to other research, 
and it is therefore more a question of the development of the classification criteria. These were 
developed extensively focusing on integration with the MATChE platform, hence the tools were 
classified to several MATChE categorizations. The value in this is that it enables identification of 
tools based on the outcome of the readiness assessment. But the final support tool also contained 
a stand-alone version, the Toolr website, which presents the backstage framework through a filte-
==PAGE:130==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 112
ring feature. This was well received in the final evaluation phase, which makes the authors believe 
that, despite the strong relationship with the MATChE platform, the final solution also has an 
autonomy and thus relevance for other netvorks. This is believed to increase the value of the tool 
for non-target groups, especially users within the secondary group. That supports the motivation 
of making research and knowledge within the area accessible to other networks than academia as 
well as providing an overview for academia about potential research gaps. 
 6.2 Conclusion 
The aim of this thesis has been to establish an overview of existing research within tools for 
circular economy and based on this, design a support tool that can aid manufacturing companies 
in identifying relevant tools. The reason for this has been two-fold: First, because the literature 
review revealed that knowledge about the implementation of circular initiatives is sparse at the 
micro level, i.e. the companies need support in order to transition. Second, because in order to re-
veal potential research gaps it was necessary to create an overview and systemise existing research. 
The first phase of the research focused on the identification of tools for circular economy. A sy-
stematic literature review based on a developed protocol containing well defined methodological 
steps was executed resulting in 49 tools relevant within the scope of the MATChE platform. The 
explicitly outlined SLR protocol allows for other professionals to be able to judge the adequacy 
of the design or reproduce the protocol. The tools derived from published literature serving to 
prescribe how to transition to circular economy and has been evaluated on several criteria. From 
the execution of the SLR it was possible to indicate potential research gaps: some dimensions 
within the circular economy could be seen to be less researched, but the most interesting was to 
see how the number of tools became less and less towards actual implementation, a finding also 
that was also stated from the literature review in Chapter 1.  
The second phase involved the systematisation of the resulting tools that could support users in 
the identification of relevant tools and thereby make the support useful. Through the develop-
ment of classification criteria and the classification of the tools according to those, a backstage 
framework was established allowing for filtering and identification of tools.
Phase 3 focused on the embodiment of the framework in order to enhance the usability of the 
support tool and make it usable for different users and user approaches. The result of this phase 
was a prototype of the final support: the website Toolr which is also integrated in my.MATChE 
through tools suggestions on the users readiness profile. Furthermore, the prototype included 
individual information pages for all of the 49 tools. These pages were developed on basis of the 
==PAGE:131==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 113
classification criteria as well as empirical data and a workshop involving co-creation with a Danish 
textile service company and by using data merging so that it is easy to keep Toolr updated.  
The last phase of the research, phase 4, focused on initial tests and evaluation of the envisioned 
support tool through the prototype. It was observed, that the systematisation of the tools enabled 
the users to identify the usefulness of tools, both in relation to the MATChE dimensions and to 
the specific company context. The evaluation of the usability of the prototype received positive 
feedback regarding utility. Furthermore, the overall scope, in relation to enabling the identification 
of tools, was confirmed as being relevant and valuable, both for target users and secondary users. 
However, it also proved necessary to undertake future iterations of the proposed support tool in 
order to enhance simplicity and coherence of the solution.  
The scientific contribution of this thesis has been an overview of existing tools for circular eco-
nomy in the scope of manufacturing companies in the form of a backstage framework containing 
49 tools from published studies developed as part of DS-2 presented in Chapter 5. 
The practical contribution of this thesis is how the classification of the tools enables companies 
to identify tools that match their objective and readiness and how the tool pages, based on the 
developed classifications criteria and features, enables the users to identify the relevance of the 
tool. The design of the support is delivered in a way that makes it easy to implement and maintain 
as part of the MATChE platform. Lastly, it should be mentioned that the design also enables data 
capture through use that can be beneficial in understanding successful roadmaps. 
 6.2.1  Limitations 
The following section includes a reflection on the limitations of the approach used in the thesis 
regarding both specific methods, as well as the overall methodology. This will be followed by sug-
gestions for further work in section 7.2. 
Scope of  the SLR 
The systematic literature review comprised only published articles and book chapters and it was 
not iterated after the final execution, nor did an execution in grey literature happen. This was not 
possible due to the extensive amount of material in relation to the time restrictions of the thesis. 
Challenges of  accessing the target users
Throughout the thesis it has proven difficult to establish contact to companies within the target 
users: manufacturing companies. This has meant that user involvement in both the PS and DS-2 
stages has been influenced by the secondary user group. However, it was possible to get a larger 
Danish production company for tests in DS2, as well as a larger Danish textile service company 
in both PS and DS2
==PAGE:132==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 114
Best practices are few
The novelty of the research field makes it difficult to retrieve research about best practices. It was 
very few of the resulting tools (only 3 out of 49) that were classified as consolidated and most of 
the best practices are probably to be found within grey literature. The result may also have been 
influenced by the design of the SLR protocol as limited to published material, as well as excluded 
material regarding very well-known tools such as LCA. 
Classification Criteria 
The classification criteria were not iterated following the development in the PS stage. This means 
that the backstage framework is not updated with the new classification criteria that were devel-
oped during this stage. This applies to value tags and images of the tools. In addition, in the DS-1 
stage there was no time to involve users to test these criteria before the systematization began.
Focus for the workshop was only at the development of the tool pages
The second iteration of the PS stage included one workshop where the focus was on the tool 
pages and not the integration and context part developed in the third iteration. This iteration did 
not include user involvement. 
 6.3 Reﬂections and further work 
This section rounds off the thesis: first with some personal reflections from the authors about 
what could have been done differently and second with a section about further work. 
 6.3.1  What could have been done differently?
It is always easy to point out what you would have done differently because the process, as often 
due to the benefit of hindsight, has created a lot of insights for improvement. It is on the other 
hand challenging to retrieve the struggles and difficulties experienced prior to these insights. 
Throughout the project the authors have struggled with the scope of the thesis and felt that the 
amount of material from the SLR process, at sometimes, was overwhelming to comprehend and 
analyse, due to the complexity of the research field. It has been a concern that the width and 
quantity of the analysis would compromise the depth and quality of the results. 
Nevertheless, the authors believe that if this thesis had only been based on certain dimensions as 
a limitation strategy, it would have lost its value. The main value within this thesis has been the 
establishment of a systematic overview of current research that was non-existent. We recognize 
==PAGE:133==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 115
that this may mean a lack of insight or quality in depth. Width versus depth is always a balan-
ce The SLR process took a long time, partly because the authors had to familiarize themselves 
with the method, while at the same time gain an understanding of the area of research. It was 
interesting to experience how it went faster in the PS stage, as more familiar methods were taken 
into use. Here, it was a personal goal for the authors to conceptualize and embody the backstage 
framework for a more comprehensive concept. Retrospectively, the scope of this phase could 
have been different. The scope could have been limited, for example to have been centered on 
the communication of tools through the tools pages or another narrower focus which could have 
improved the results of the evaluation in the last phase. Furthermore, the focus of this stage, 
more or less consciously, took a turn about the feasibility of the concept, initiated by the way the 
authors assessed the developed features. Looking back, this focus might not be so interesting as 
the realization that the prototype was not as important, at this level of detail, but instead served 
to facilitate a conversation about the concept. 
The last reflection regarding the process relates to user involvement. It has been unfamiliar not 
to involve users early in the project, since early user recruitment has been a distinct approach 
to projects through the authors' education. A major consideration was to involve the MATChE 
users in the development of classification criteria in DS2, for example, through an early test with 
a pretotype, but due to time limitations it was not possible. However, it was experienced, that with 
CE, user involvement relates to the famous quote “If I had asked people what they wanted, they 
would have said faster horses” said by Henry Ford. We believe that the overview provided by this 
thesis and the function of the prototype as a boundary object helps to facilitate a conversation 
with the users now, that was not possible without, which can be of great help for the iterations 
needed in order to enhance the support tool.
 6.3.2  Further work 
During the completion of this thesis, the authors have often discussed the perspectives of a fol-
lowing project. 
The thesis has been limited by its timeframe and scope and ends at a conceptual stage. As part of 
the delimitation of the thesis, the DS-2 stage has been on very initial basis and therefor the final 
solution for support is not iterated and some of the future work is already presented in Chapter 
5 as part of the evaluation. This part proposes a number of initiatives that can be seized in order 
for improvements of the research and its outcomes, and for the application of the support tool. 
These suggestions are described below. 
Gray literature 
An execution within gray literature could definitely be interesting in order to expand the back-
stage framework. It would mean that the medium of the support tool should relate to this input 
being different than the existing content only being published literature. But since the material 
from gray literature probably is more understandable for companies compared to the ‘translation’ 
==PAGE:134==
INTRO
METHOD
DS-I
PS
DS-II
OUTRO
page 116
needed on academic literature, the work load should not be as heavy as with academic literature. 
However, it is more difficult to execute a SLR within gray literature and thereby ensure the trans-
parency regarding the methodological steps behind the framework. A solution to this should be 
considered, so that the database retains its validity and does not end up, as some of the current 
platforms, with an overwhelming material and primarily functions as link repositories.
Mapping inter-relation between tools
Mapping the inter-relation between the tools in the backstage framework could be interesting in 
order to study similarities and differences. Additionally, it could provide the possibility for users 
to compare tools. 
Iteration of  SLR within tool gaps 
The analysis of the execution found that two dimensions and two subdimensions was found to 
be sparsely populated with tools and one subdimension had zero tools related. A new SLR or 
improvement of the existing search-string with an extended set of keywords could potentially 
lead to a different result or investigate whether the missing tools is due to a gap within research. 
Integration with MATChE
The integration of Toolr with the MATChE platform should be iterated and developed with 
the focus on creating coherence between the two platforms, since it from the tests was explored 
how the users experienced a lack of simplicity and coherence in the prototype. This could both 
be improved through a change in layout, but also whether tool suggestions should be part of the 
user's readiness profile or should be suggested otherwise. Furthermore, it should be investigated 
whether there is an appropriate number of tools suggested to the user and thus if the current 
solution is sufficient, where tools are suggested based on dimension, subdimension and current 
readiness level as well as the next level. It could especially be interesting to look into the suggesti-
on of best-fit tools and continue to improve the customisation and sophistication within the way 
the backstage framework generates suggestions. 
Common language 
Academia and industry are two different networks and if research on tools for CE is to flow bet-
ween these two networks as a boundary object(s), it needs to reflect the language of each network 
in order to create a sufficient degree of meaningfulness. The tools need to be presented in a man-
ner that makes it possible for companies to identify and comprehend, at least, the core value of 
the tool. This proved to be successful to a certain degree in the thesis, but further work is needed 
to engage and involve companies in order to advance create a common language and enhance the 
communication used in the support.

Appendix 1
Activity planning
Activity planning 
Timeline and overall planning of the thesis project has been structured four development stages, Research Clar-
ification(identification), Descriptive studies 1(systematisation), Prescriptive studies(development) and De-
scriptive studies 2(test and evaluation), to progress and keep track Milestone and Midterm meetings have been 
used as stage-gates.  In the original planning the stages were planned as work-packages, this was changed later to 
Phases with the adoption of the Design Research Methodology along with the names of the stages.
Daily and weekly planning 
was handled with sticky-notes 
on a two-week plan, assisted 
by the overall project plan, 
restructuring was done if 
needed on the overall plan.

Appendix 2.1
MATChE assessment questions
30 MATChE Assessment Questions 
During the MATChE assessment the user will answer all 30 questions(see list). The questions will be 
answered on the five point readiness scale with two additional options shown below. 
1.
Understanding the potential 
2.
Planning pilot implementation 
3.
Piloting initiatives 
4.
Planning scale-up 
5.
Scaling up initiatives 
6.
Don’t know 
7.
Not relevant for my company 
 
 ​MATChE Assessment Questions 
1.
Organisation 
1.1.
Knowledge & Skills -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready in terms of 
knowledge and skills regarding Circular Economy? 
1.2.
Risk & Investment -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to take risks 
and invest? 
1.3.
Processes & Tools - ​To what degree are your processes and tools ready to support the 
change to Circular Economy? 
1.4.
Business Case - ​To what degree is your company/business unit ready to develop a clear 
business case for implementing new initiatives? 
2.
Strategy and business model innovation 
2.1.
New Value Proposition -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to 
identify new value proposition and possibilities across the product life cycle?  
2.2.
Long-term Strategy -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to embrace 
circular economy in your long-term strategy? 
2.3.
New Revenue Streams -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to define 
new revenue streams and financial models (e.g. resell products)?  
2.4.
Resources -​ To what degree are your managers ready to commit to initiatives and allocate 
resources? 
2.5.
Communication -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to communicate 
the value of new offerings to the market? 
3.
Product and service innovation 
3.1.
Product/Service-Systems -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to 
develop and deliver Products/Service-Systems? 
3.2.
Design for Life Extension -​ To what degree are your company’s products ready for 
extended lifetime (design for maintenance, modularity, change of spare parts, etc.)?  
3.3.
Design for End-of-Life -​ To what degree are your company’s products ready for 
End-of-Life (design for disassembly, remanufacturing, recycling, etc.)? 
3.4.
Design for Sharing -​ To what degree are your company’s products and services ready to 
be shared with other users? 
 
 
==PAGE:149==
page 131
4.
Manufacturing and value chain 
4.1.
New Partnerships -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to establish 
new partnerships in the value chain? 
4.2.
Supply Chain -​ To what extent is your company/business unit ready to collaborate with 
and/or influence your suppliers to encourage circular initiatives in your upstream supply 
chain?  
4.3.
Materials in Manufacturing -​ To what degree is your company ready for the use of 
recycled/renewable/biodegradable materials in manufacturing processes? 
4.4.
Industrial Symbiosis -​ To what degree is your value chain and manufacturing ready for 
industrial symbiosis (e.g. using own or 3rd parties’ by-products as resource)? 
5.
Use, support and maintenance 
5.1.
Service Support -​ To what degree are your services ready for product support during use 
phase (e.g. maintenance, consultancy, advice, etc.)? 
5.2.
Repair Service -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to repair products 
so to extend their lifetime? 
5.3.
Sharing Platforms -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to establish 
sharing platforms which can encourage shared product use and access? 
6.
Technology and data 
6.1.
Monitoring During Use -​ To what degree is your technology ready for product 
monitoring during the use phase (e.g. Internet of Things (IoT), Big Data, etc.)? 
6.2.
Technology for Extended Use - ​To what degree is your technology ready to support the 
product for extended lifetime and handle the product at End-of-Life (e.g. via 3D printing 
of new spare parts)?  
7.
Take back and end-of-use strategies 
7.1.
Take back systems -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to establish 
take-back systems for products after their use? 
7.2.
Disassembly & Remanufacturing -​To what degree is your company/business unit ready 
to disassemble and remanufacture products? 
7.3.
Materials Recycling -​ To what degree is your company/business unit ready to recover 
the value out of products at End-of-Life, through material recycling? 
7.4.
Market for 2nd-Life Products -​ To what degree is your company familiar with the 
market readiness for second-life products (e.g. remanufactured, reused or recycled 
products)? 
8.
Policy and market 
8.1.
Market for New Business Models -​ To what degree is your company familiar with the 
market readiness for new business models (e.g. leasing instead of selling)? 
8.2.
Market for Co-development -​ To what degree is your company familiar with the 
potential for the co-development of new solutions with key stakeholders? 
8.3.
Sectoral legislation -​ To what degree is your company familiar with the sectorial 
legislative frameworks that influence the implementation of Circular Economy initiatives? 
8.4.
Inter-/national Legislation - ​To what degree is your company familiar with the national 
and international legislative framework that influence the implementation of Circular 
Economy initiatives?

Appendix 4.1
Systematic Literature Review protocol
The aim of the protocol is to guide the execution of a systematic literature review and a definition of the 
objectives and criteria for the evaluation, selection and analysis of the following results of the review.  
 
The protocol has been developed as part of the master thesis project “Enhancement of Circular Economy 
readiness through best practices and tools - the case of the MATChE project” 
 
1. Definition of the research focus  
 
Question Focus: 
Identification and systematization of tools and methods to support circular economy development in 
Danish manufacturing companies.  
 
The systematization will support the MATChE assessment tool by relating the tools to where in the 
transition the users are based on the 8 dimensions and thereby enhance their ability to implement CE.  
 
 
Question Quality and Amplitude 
 
Problem​: 
CE is an umbrella concept that touches on a broad field of research and competencies and provides 
companies with a lot of opportunities to develop their business as well as increase value and productivity 
while ensuring a sustainable future.The MATChE assessment tool will, based on a score, identify gaps and 
opportunities for companies where to start the transition. This is based on 8 dimensions which broadly 
covers across circular stages and organizational focus.  
 
There are a myriad of tools and methods to support and plan these options, but potential users may find it 
difficult to identify the right tool. There lacks a comprehensive and systematic framework that can provide 
an overview of tools related to where companies see opportunities for development.  
 
 
Question​:  
  
What tools are presented that aid the transition towards a Circular Economy in the scope of danish 
manufacturing companies?  
 
Intervention​:  
We search for tools that can support danish manufacturing companies in Circular Economy transition 
 
==PAGE:154==
page 136
Appendix 4.2
Systematic Literature Review protocol
Contro​l:  
None 
 
Effect​:  
Tools (see keyword B) that are applicable to the target audience 
 
Outcome Measure​:  
Number of identified tools  
 
Population​:  
Scientific Journal Publications and book chapters relating to CE  
 
Application​:  
The users of the MATChE platform, the MATChE team at DTU and in general researchers/consultants 
within CE. 
 
Experimental design​:  
No statistical method is going to be applied.

4. Definition of criteria  
 
Studies definition  
 
1.
Qualification criteria for selected studies  
1.1.
Only journal papers and book chapters are selected to be analyzed 
 
2.
Inclusion criteria for selected studies  
2.1.
Subject area must be of relevance for the Danish manufacturing industry i.e. ​relevant for 
the MATChE target users  
2.2.
Focus of article should be on Micro- or Meso- level, i.e. company or regional level 
2.3.
Article must present a ​prescriptive​ tool/method/guide to solve a strategic, practical or 
implementation problem 
2.4.
The presented tool must be validated through at least a case study  
2.5.
A tool specific to a technology or area should show potential for application on a 
more general matter   
==PAGE:158==
page 140
3.1.
Subject area out of scope  
3.2.
The article concerns the topic in a ​descriptive​ manner  
3.3.
If the focus of the article is on a macro level 
3.4.
Geographically out of scope 
3.5.
Articles that only shows the implementation of a well known tool and the results there of  
3.6.
Article information is not presented in a succinct manner i.e. Clearly and understandable 
communicated  
3.7.
If the article is too narrowly focused on a specific sector or technology  
3.8.
In the method is too narrowly focused area  
 
4.
Studies types definition 
Secondary literature in forms of: Journal articles and Book chapters 
 
5.
Procedures for studies selection 
The search strings must be run at the selected sources. To select an initial set of studies the search 
string is executed in Scopus and Web Of Science searching within journal articles and book 
chapters.  
 
The selection procedures are as following: 
 
- Studies selection procedure 1: Reading of title, abstract and keywords;  
- Studies selection procedure 2: Reading of introduction and conclusion;  
- Studies selection procedure 3: Reading full paper. 
 
All of the studies will be evaluated according to inclusion and exclusion criteria to select an final set 
of primary studies. On basis of the results obtained an evaluation regarding executing searches 
within grey literature will be discussed. 
 
The protocol needs to be approved by experts and by testing in order to start execution. This will be done 
with supervisors and experts Tim McAloone and Daniela Pigosso.   
3.
Exclusion criteria for selected studies

Systematic Literature Review protocol

For SP2 and SP3 the included studies will be stored in Mendeley and read there. This will be documented in 
a separate Google Sheet spreadsheet.  
 
The result of all selection procedures will be explicit in each of the spreadsheets in order to allow 
backtracking.  
5. Analysis and results presentation   
The results of SLR will be: state of art regarding tools for CE transition applicable for Danish 
manufacturing companies and a backstage framework containing the tools extracted from the studies.  
 
Quantitative information about the SLR process are collected for example the amount of studies found in 
each database, amount of overlaps between the databases, results of each selection procedure, amount of 
studies per year etc.  
 
The tools found in the studies are extracted and stored in a spreadsheet, a backstage framework. Each tools 
must have the following information: ID number, Article/Book chapter title, name of the tool and a short 
description. Further classifications will be developed and this process will be documented in the thesis.  
4. Documentation of Execution  
The studies are stored both in ੔Google Sheets​ and in the Reference Manager software  ੔Mendeley​. Each study 
will be assigned an ID number in order to identify the study across spreadsheets and for communication.  
For selection procedure 1 (SP1), the information will be stored in Google Sheets, using Scopus’ output style 
data, and the abstract will be read from here.

Appendix 10
Final set of studies (47 articles)

==PAGE:171==
page 153
Appendix 11.1
Research gaps
 
Tool gaps on cluster level 
 
Looking at the tool distribution divided into the four clusters and compared with the overall tool 
distribution in red no significant deviations are found. In the dimensions with low coverage especially 
cluster 1 and 2 have low support in dimension 5 and have no supporting tools in dimension 6. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:172==
page 154
At sub-dimension level the distribution is replicated but in higher detail, cluster 3 is generally the cluster 
with best coverage. 
 
Appendix 11.2
Research gaps
==PAGE:173==
page 155
Looking at readiness there is an equal distribution of application.  
 
 
The general distribution of tools for clusters show a very varians of five tools from the lowest to the highest.   
 
 
 
 
Appendix 11.3
Research gaps
==PAGE:174==
page 156
Information gathered from the classifying criteria  
The tool types identified in the classification are mainly of the type Framework and Guideline, Simulation 
and Analysis model are the next most frequent types found. 
 
 
Three criteria were rated on a High / Low scale and the distribution can be seen in the Tool information 
graph. Most tools were rated as Low in all three, and in Application and acquisition cost only two were 
classified as High. 
 
 
 
Appendix 11.4
Research gaps
==PAGE:175==
page 157
Tool Input / Output type more tools were found to focus on Quantitative data.  
 
 
Only 30% of the tool were found to described in a superficial manner, this means that 70% of the tools 
should be described in enough detail and clarity for an informed user to understand and start to use the tool 
based on reading the article describing then tool. 
 
 
94% of tools were evaluated to be in the earlier phases of development.

Park J.Y., Chertow 
M.R.
Establishing and testing the 
'reuse potential' indicator for 
managing wastes as resources
Reuse potential 
indicator
A ''reuse potential indicator'' to specify how 
''resource-like'' versus how ''waste-like'' specific 
materials are on a continuum in order to support 
the decision making process. Reuse potential is 
expressed as a real value between 0 and 1, where 
0 represents ''waste'' and 1 presents ''ressource''. 
The reuse potential quantifies the technical 
maximum reusability according to the technologies 
that are economically available to reuse the 
material in an environmentally sound way.

Appendix 13.1
Classification criteria evaluation and definition
Classification criteria candidates 
All candidate criteria were evaluated on three points. 
●
What: The aim of the criteria 
●
Relevance: How the criteria was relevant to the user or project 
●
How: Describing how the data could be retrieved from the article 
 
 
1.
MATChE Dimension link 
1.1.
What​: How the tool relates to MATChE Circular Economy business dimension 
1.2.
Relevance​: Link tools directly with MATChE readiness assessment, to suggest tools for all 
subdimensions 
1.3.
How​: Evaluate using the MATChE assessment questions(see appendix 4 30 MATChE 
assessment questions) if the tool has relevance in supporting the sub dimension 
 
2.
MATChE Cluster relevance 
2.1.
What​: Is the tool generic or sector specific, link to relevant clusters  
2.2.
Relevance​: Sorting possible to exclude tools not relevant to issues in the user context 
(direct link to the MATCHE platform and the user's profile) 
2.3.
How​: Evaluate on the application area of the tool, can it be used more generic than 
described 
2.3.1.
Food(not Agriculture), Textile, Paper, Wood 
2.3.2.
Pharma, Chemistry, Plastic 
2.3.3.
Transport, Machinery, Metal, Electronic 
2.3.4.
Furniture, Building (only looking at component level) 
 
3.
Readiness level of tool 
3.1.
What​: In what development/readiness phases the tool has relevant application.  
3.2.
Relevance​: Should a company have x level of maturity before using this tool? The 
readiness scale is defined from how companies processes are when implementing 
something new 
3.2.1.
Understanding the potential 
3.2.2.
Planning pilot implementation 
3.2.3.
Piloting initiatives 
3.2.4.
Planning scale-up 
3.2.5.
Scaling up initiatives 
3.3.
How​: Evaluate in what part of the process the tool has application and could be 
prescriptive

How​: Is the tool focused on a single process or has more company wide implications  
16.3.1.
Narrow (single process or procedure) 
16.3.2.
Wide (org change or business model reinvention) 
16.4.

17.2.
Relevance​: Understanding the level of use 
17.3.

17.4.

to granulate application level. / is supply chain not just between micro and meso?

33
Value recovery from end-of-
use products facilitated by 
automated dismantling 
planning
End-Of-Use 
value recovery 
plan
A two-stage dismantling planning 
method to find a profitable end-of-
use strategy. 
First stage, disassembly optimization 
model is constructed to allowing for 
maximum preservation of 
component function value. 
Second stage, analysis is used to 
increase profitability and identify 
bottlenecks to disassembly. A hard 
disk drive serves as a case study to 
illustrate the method.

Product-level 
circularity metric

as a basis for aggregating

enable customers and producers to 
contribute systematically to an 
increased degree of material 
recirculation.

352
Establishing and testing the 
'reuse potential' indicator for 
managing wastes as resources
Reuse potential 
indicator
A ''reuse potential indicator'' to 
specify how ''resource-like'' versus 
how ''waste-like'' specific materials 
are on a continuum in order to 
support the decision making 
process. Reuse potential is 
expressed as a real value between 0 
and 1, where 0 represents ''waste'' 
and 1 presents ''ressource''. The 
reuse potential quantifies the 
technical maximum reusability 
according to the technologies that 
are economically available to reuse 
the material in an environmentally 
sound way.

Longevity 
indicator
The longevity indicator measures 
contribution to material retention 
based on the amount of time a 
resource is kept in use which can be 
applied at managerial and 
organizational levels to measure the 
impact of business decisions on the 
longevity of precious materials.

Appendix 14.7

==PAGE:191==
page 173
Appendix 15.1
Guide to the Backstage Framework
Guide to the use of the Backstage framework 
The backstage framework is a database of tools and methods that can support manufacturing
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
companies that wish implement Circular Economy principles and secure their place in the new
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
resource economy.  
Through a MATChE readiness assessment or using framework’s filters, recommendations of
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relevant tools in 30 business dimensions will be made. The recommendations are based on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
researched Circular economy success factors all based on company readiness competencies and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
business focus. 
 
Tools will be presented in a way that makes it easier to understand the relevance for your company.
 
 
 
     
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
A single page has been complied with information about the tool and when in a implementation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
process it can provide guidance, all this to help you decide if its the right match for you. 
 
To get the optimal value from the backstage framework it is recommended to access the MATChE
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
platform and perform a free readiness assessment. The assessment will give you an understanding
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of your company’s competencies, strengths and opportunities in eight manufacturing relevant
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Circular Economy business dimensions. 
 
The stand alone version of the backstage framework allow filtering of the tools in various ways. 
Based on  
-
Four business segment clusters 
-
Eight business dimensions or 30 subdimensions 
-
The application area of the tool, from support in ‘understanding the potential’ to ‘scaling
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
up initiatives’  
-
7 other filters like ‘Application and acquisitions cost’ or ‘Demanded time for use’ and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
‘input/output data’ are also ready to be explored. 
 
Using the backstage framework 
Applying filters in the backstage framework file (stand alone) 
1. Open ToolrBackstage in google sheets at ​http://tiny.cc/hrbuuy (download a kopi or use
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
online) 
2. Select the Backstage tab  
3. On the green Filters row, click the filter icon on the area your with to filter by, unscelect
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
“empty” and press OK. Only tools relevant to this area are now present in the list below. 
4. Additional filter can be added or removed by re-selecting “empty” on the filters. 
5. Example: find a tool to help pilot initiatives in designing for end-of-life in the metal
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
industry. - Set filters in ​Piloting initiatives + ​Design for end-of-life + ​Cluster
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Transport, Machinery, Metal & Electronic​. Only relevant tools are now left, review the
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
tool description for tools that could be of interest. 
==PAGE:192==
page 174
Updating the Toolr backstage framework with new tools 
 
The process of identifying and adding new tools to the Toolr backstage framework follow two 
overall steps. First, defining if the paper is within scope, Second, analyzing and classifying the tool 
into the backstage framework. 
  
The first step is very suitable for updates of the framework by updating the systematic literature 
review with new research published after the last search was performed. 
  
The second step where a tool is analyzed and classified in the backstage framework can be used if a 
paper describing a tool is found to comply with the selection inclusion and exclusion criteria*. 
 
Defining if a paper is within scope. 
The article must comply with the selection inclusion and exclusion criteria* defined in the SLR 
protocol 
If analyzing a larger number of possible candidate papers the below process can be used 
1.    Read title, abstract, keywords of paper if they give indication of compliance with criteria go to 
step 2 if not exclude paper. 
2.    Read introduction and conclusion of paper if this give indication of compliance with criteria 
go to step 3 if not exclude paper. 
3.    Read full paper, if the paper complies with criteria analyze and classify the paper and tool 
found. 
 
Appendix 15.2
Guide to the Backstage Framework
==PAGE:193==
page 175
Analysis and classifying. 
If a paper or tool complies with the selection inclusion and exclusion criteria* then it can be added 
to the backstage framework and become part of the tools recommended for users. 
To classify a tool, follow these instructions: 
1.    Open the backstage framework 
2.    Open tab “Article metadata” Enter or import (see import) the papers meta data on last line and 
corresponding to the column title. Give the paper the next ID number (last ID number +1) 
3.    Go to “Tool Classification” tab. Enter the ID number and title of the paper after the last tool, 
in column A and B. 
4.    Go through the paper and use the classification criteria in in top as a guide. All the fields have 
notes further explaining the criteria, and they are described along with motivation in the report 
6.2 Description of final classification criteria. If a Readiness level, sub dimensions or clusters is 
deemed relevant, mark the corresponding cell with a “1”.  
5.    Tool information classification have drop down list that must be selected from, find the best 
match. For more information refer the report 6.2 Description of final classification criteria 
where they are described along with motivation and reviewer guiding. 
6.    Identify name of tool and write a short easy readable text. The text should describe what the 
tool does and if possible state something about in what case or context it was tested. 
7.    If possible save an image or screenshot of the tool, save the image with the tool ID and file 
extension .png, under the tool marge image folder. 
 
*Import base metadata from paper.  
If the paper has been found on an academic database search engine like Scopus or Web of Science, 
data can be downloaded using export forms on the site. Include relevant information like title, 
abstract, authors, publication journal, year, citation, link to article, author keywords, index 
keywords. 
 
 
 
Appendix 15.3
Guide to the Backstage Framework
==PAGE:194==

Appendix 16.1
Exploring exsisting platforms
Exploring existing platforms 
This appendix contains a description of the plat-
forms that were selected for the market research 
and pictures from the session. The various plat-
forms' features were evaluated in a post-it session 
in terms of content, user experience and maturity 
level of content. 
“Circular Design Guide”
The Circular Design Guide is a collaboration bet-
ween the Ellen MacArthur Foundation and IDEO 
with worksheets, workshop material and videos de-
veloped to the platform that can aid companies in 
understanding the potential and implementation of 
circular economy. Furthermore, the platform contains 
links and case studies. 
“Cirkulær Virksomhed”
CirkulærVirksomhed.dk is a platform run by The Da-
nish Environmental Protection Agency. 
The portal contains tools, examples of circular com-
panies, and links to other websites that all help inspire 
and challenge businesses to become more circular.
==PAGE:195==
page 177
Appendix 16.2
Exploring exsisting platforms
“Circulator”
The Circulator is a project funded by EIT Raw Ma-
terials, a body of EU, aimed at supporting busines-
ses and entrepreneurs in making conscious strategic 
choices regarding the sustainability of their business 
model and value proposition.
The platform offers a web-based tool providing an 
overview of the most relevant business models for the 
raw materials industry in the context of the circular 
economy, as well as relevant case studies.
“CE Guide”
CE Guide is mainly a link repository that enables an 
effective search for tools, case studies and other re-
sources with the purpose of providing guidance for 
the CE transition. 
“ResCom”
ResCoM, which stands for Resource Conservative 
Manufacturing, is a project managed by EMF which 
has developed a collection of methodologies and 
tools for the implementation of closed-loop manufac-
turing systems. Furthermore a lot of case studies and 
publicated material is available through the platform. 
“SHIFT”
SHIFT is a cross-sector collaboration between MIT 
and Valutus and mainly an online platform that al-
lows the navigation and identiﬁcation of sustainabi-
lity tools that exist around the internet and within the 
industry. Furthermore, SHIFT facilitates a community 
of practitioners whereas it is possible to review tools 
based on experiences.
==PAGE:196==
page 178
Appendix 16.3
Exploring exsisting platforms
“Embedding project”
Embedding project is a research project consisting 
of different stakeholders within industry, research, te-
chnology and regulatory agencies and runned by a 
dedicated team.

Appendix 18.1
Interview 1
After participating in the conference "Driving design” by IDA (18th of January 2018) we arranged a 
meeting with ​Circular architect firm​ who held a lecture there.  
The motivation for talking to firm was that they are highly recognized in the industry as narrators of circular 
stories. 
 
Date:​ 23/1/2018 
Medium:​ ​In person 
Language: ​Danish 
Participants: 
Nanna la Cour Sonne (Student - Technical University of Denmark) 
Jeppe Sloth Olsen (Student - Technical University of Denmark) 
Participant A - DV (Managing Partner - Circular architect firm ) 
Participant B - MF (Consultant - Circular architect firm ) 
Type of interview: 
informal, semi-structured  
 
Questions: 
●
How do you understand CE and what development do you experience within the field of CE? 
●
What tools/methods do you use when consulting companies?  
●
How do you handle knowledge sharing between projects? 
●
What issues do companies need your help for?/ What are the problems customers are struggling 
with when they come to you?  
●
What are the customers lacking in skills that you can offer?  
 
 
Key findings:  
●
That the world around CE is complex and that CE can be seen more as a philosophy than a defined 
(/undefined) concept.  
●
CE is interdisciplinary work with a lot of different stakeholders engaged and it’s important to enable the 
facilitation of knowledge sharing.   
●
Lendager is approached by customers because of different causes;  
they want to implement CE on an operation level, wish to get help with a specific waste problem, 
product etc., or want to engage and learn how to design/upcycle/ reduce waste.  
●
A lot of their work consists in telling the good story and get companies to understand and see the 
potential in CE and that it is actually a durable and profitable way of running a business.  
●
On a strategic level with the board of director to help them see the potential in CE, that it’s more than 
recycling and waste, Trend forecasting, help them understand and map their own possibilities and 
understand the limits to what makes sens.  
business opportunities vs. effort 
 
 
Quotes: 
1.
“​It is important not just to try to precisely define what CE is, but to think of it as a philosophy”’ 
 
2.
“We see CE as the agent, not the target” 
 
==PAGE:200==
page 182
3.
“Companies come to us either with a specific problem related to ressources or waste or because they want to 
engage in the transition to Circular Economy”  
 
4.
About how they consult companies: 
“​On a strategic level we engage with the board of director to help them see the potential in CE and that it’s 
more than recycling and waste. Then we do trend forecasting, help them understand and map their own 
possibilities and understand the limits to what makes sense in their specific case​” 
 
5.
“​We use this diagram (business opportunities vs. effort, red.) to help the see where they are either not ready, 
where the low hanging fruits are, where the long term innovation potential is and where the thing in 
between is; the sweet spot.” 
 
6.
About knowledge sharing: 
“We have a really flat organizational structure where it’s easy to consult each other for inspiration and 
knowledge and have a very interdisciplinary approach”  
 
7.
“Many clients come in the door with the notion that CE is just the next sustainability and is all about 
reduce, reuse and recycle​” 
 
8.
“​We removed the “customer relation” from our Business Model and replaced it with a ‘risks and barriers’ 
because we see our customers more as partners since all CE activities brings us closer to them.​”  
 
9.
“We are engaged with our customers on all stages to implementation. Sometimes we work with them 
throughout the process, sometimes only strategic or practical.​”

into companies

CRS and Quality management are the typical people looking for inspiration. 
●
Tools needs to communicated in a very graphical way, if it’s too academic it will be left on the shelf. 
 
Quotes 
1.
I:who in the company takes the initiative to seek and work with new methods to go circular? 
R: “​It’s very different in each cage who takes the initiative to work circular, but one think we can see 
from our projects is that projects that are anchored in leadership are much more likely to succeed.” 
2.
I:What is the main value that they expect from there work, is there more than branding? 
R: ​“We should really try to mode away from it being just about marketing and greenwashing(just for 
show). If you think marketing as part of your business model then it can make sense, “If we succeed in 
getting the management/CEO to understand the economic potential of this and that they can profit on 
the bottom line. that is why we takt CE and not sustainability it’s just easier that way. Then they are 
willing to work with this(CE) and can then use it as part of their marketing.” ​[requirement: value 
tag] 
3.
R: ​What a lot of companies realise is that they need a lot of data on their products. 3xR møbler that 
buy used furniture, refurbish it and resell it has data on everything they do. 
“the companies that are not born circular, have realised that it’s a long term strategy, it’s about risk 
management, but the business side always have to be there. The first thing company owners ask us 
when we arrive is, “is there money in it”.“ 
4.
I: ​What type of persons in the company starts of looking for new tools / process and why 
5.
R: The management link is important but that is when the company has decided to work with CE, 
we meet them at conferences.

Appendix 24.1
Interview 7
Date:​ 10/5/2018 
Medium:​ ​Phone 
Language: ​Danish 
Participants: 
Jeppe Sloth Olsen (Student - Technical University of Denmark) 
Participant E - MM​ (Business development in production industry - Regional development) 
Type of interview: 
informal, semi-structured  
 
Question focus 
●
Do companies seek tool/methods themselves 
●
What barriers are experienced? 
●
Who initiates the search for tools? 
●
Types of personas, question then validate ( owner manager / CSR / tech Specialist / Consultant ) 
●
Types of scenarios, question then validate  
 
Key findings 
●
The tool can be something the company filles out together with a consultant, were the concepts 
can be elaborated. 
●
The companies need hand holding, they do not necessary understand the questions the right way 
and may therefor answer incorrectly 
●
It’s a strong feature if they can write in their own suggestions for solving or advancing a problem 
area, this creates “buy-in” and responsibility for the next steps.  
●
Creating an “easy win” feeling, make sure not to overcomplicate the solution, if they get stuck or 
overwhelmed they may leave and hesitate to come back. Focus on what is important, help them 
prioritise and show them how to progress(take the next step)  
●
There are two worlds(domains), one in the office were a framework seems the right way to solve a 
problem and one on the production floor were the focus is to put out the current fire and give 
value in that moment, what can you do now, a list of items that people can start on now. 
●
We need to as much as possible build a bridge between the two domains, this we try to do with our 
classification tool, by translating research into a language that makes more sense in businesses 
●
Companies understand value can be created in three ways. new market potential(marketing, new 
business, better service), risk management/removal(legal, future legislation), savings(optimization, 
waste reduction) - many times they are linked so a saving may open up new business potential by 
allowing a lower price point.  
 
Quotes (in DK) 
1.
Symbiosis workers but it’s mostly internally in companies,outside with partnerships is much more 
demanding. 
2.
Environmental work is a lot about compliance and risk management​[value tag feature] 
3.
(We) ...inspire and help companies to start working (with environmental work) but we are not 
consultants  
4.
Many of the companies we work with have over 100 employees in the industry cluster, it’s them 
that have the resources to allocate/dedicate a employ for it(environmental work)  
5.
we also have some small consultants (in the cluster) but it’s a small part  
==PAGE:210==
page 192
Appendix 24.2
Interview 7
6.
Even in the larger companies it’s a lot about meeting demands, that is the market driver, that they 
are ISO certified. We make a small certificate that they can get, when they work B2B and they can 
show the certificate then they can progress to the next point and start talking about price  
7.
They want to know the easy way to live up to the demands(legislation) you would be surprised how 
low a priority it is in many companies even given their size.  
8.
It’s something of an motivation task convincing them that it’s not just a need to have but that there 
is also a potential in it. 
9.
It’s about the bottom line, about optimization.  
10. Environmental employees job is a lot about putting out fires every day, they sign up for an event 
but then cancel in the last moment because something just came up that required their attention. 
(so) ...instead of being prepared and planning ahead so they can pick the low hanging fruit, like the 
front runners with a handle on things and backing from management, instead they run around like 
headless chickens.  
11. When we try to talk CE with them, then they say “yes, but the kettle has just broke so I have no 
time to discuss CE” 
12. IT has been valuable that they had to do the test every other year, it made sure that they got the 
time from management, or they would have been kicked out, and on that background they have 
been able to keep the focus on their environmental effort.  
13. Today it’s an market parameter to have focus on environmental efforts 
14. There is a long way from the environmental employee to the management and a lot of places it 
seems that it’s just the youngest man that gets the job  
15. He(grundfos environmental manager) never came with a suggestion unless he could show 
management that it payed it self back within a year and a half, and he was driving through projects. 
16. You are also limited by the road in through environmental but it ends up being about optimizing 
and efficiency.  
17. [Personas]: IF you want to work with the part of CE that has to do with business models and other, 
then you need another way in, you need to talk to the business developer, the production leader, 
the finance or sales directors.  
18. If you are talking to the CEO then it takes 20 minutes if they see the idea till they say yes and set 
things in action. Other places it takes the environmental employee a year to get one small project of 
the ground and it’s all up hill. 
19. I don’t think we have a single company that has a employee with the title of CSR, it’s something 
that you only see en very large companies [personas] 
20. THe CSR element is growing in it(the environmental efforts)  
21. It could be the sales director that you can talk to if it’s not the business developer, they know what 
is happening in the market(new CE/CSR market needs)  
22. [Requirements] Be able to identify the projects that benefit on the market potential, it’s all about 
the bottom line. (DK:Få identificerede de projekter der giver dem markedspotentialer, det handler 
om bundlinje 
23. Value chain  (DK:værdikæde:  
○
Business Potential (DK:forretningsmuligheder),  
○
Damage control / risks,  
○
Savings(optimizing, goods in use, end of life) DK:(optimering, vare i brugsfasen, 
bortskaffelse)  
24. The big international corporations set the requirements and then it moves down through the value 
chain and it’s here they meet it 
==PAGE:211==
page 193
Appendix 24.3
Interview 7
25. You really get surprised, knowing Business Model Canvas, companies are really terrible at knowing 
their own Value proposition.  
26. When a medium size company was bidding for a large contract with a very large company, the 
customer sendt people to interview their employees about their values and work ethics, this part 
accounted for 60% and the price only 40% in the bidding. 
27. Thoses that deliver to the large also need to set demands to their subcontractors  
28. Model from novozymes(claus stig pedersen): lowest Level is  
○
1. damage control, (inactive) 
○
2. compliance(overhold regler), (reactive) 
○
3. generate savings (active) 
○
4. Market differentiating, (proactive) 
○
5. It’s your purpose to contribute to sustainability (integral), if you are at this level then 
you are in a very strong position and can bring this to your customers, so when they buy 
from you they are themself becoming more sustainable.   
29. Can we show that a tool supports these things  
30. There are a lot of companies that would not be able to see through these projects even if there is a 
good business case in it. 
31. There is a point there that they meet barriers when they try to work with this, so they really need 
support. 
32. You can feed pigs with tools, it’s about getting them used  
33. It’s a nice goal if you can start with a screening and find what could be relevant, how they move on 
from there you should not expect them to do on their own. don’t expect them to sit and play with 
it, they just don’t have the time, it’s tools that their consultants, their network and sector promoters 
can use as a frame to talk to them and maybe go on to implement it the tool supports that. 
34. Don’t get an idea that they(companies) will go and get the tools themselves and start using them “it 
ain’t going to happen”.  
35. We use to have a questionnaire that we took with us to companies and filled it in together, at every 
point they could input how they could work with this point to solve it.  at the end they could 
compile a report were all their input was in and it was really strong that is was their own solutions 
to their problems.  
36. It’s a good idea to ask them to evaluate where the value is. 
37. The sharp thing was that they did the writing, it was their own words and that it was interactive so 
they could see a development and use it as an action plan. But they could not write it themselves, 
they needed to be guided or they would have misunderstood it.  
38. CE is a new way of talking about sustainability.  
39. Scenarier: ejerleder, hvis de kan tilbyde mindre energi,  
40. It’s booming with UN’s SDG’s and many have their focus on that, but then it may suddently 
become a tool about how to achieve UN’s goals not CE. 
41. It would be cool with some small icons that showed how it contributed with potential, risk 
management, savings.

Interview 8
Date:​ 4/05/2018 
Medium:​ ​Phone 
Language: ​Danish 
Participants: 
Jeppe Sloth Olsen (Student - Technical University of Denmark) 
Participant I - SA (CSR-coordinator - Large textile washing and service company​) 
Type of interview: 
informal, semi-structured  
 
Question focus 
1.
Who initiates the search for tools? 
2.
What barriers are experienced? 
3.
What UX/UI requirements   
4.
How do you understand the value of CE, short-/long-term 
5.
How do you plan or prepare projects? 
6.
H​ow does your company seek new knowledge(tool/methods)? 
 
Key findings 
●
Information generally comes from network partners and projects 
●
Technical staff are more likely to research tools or methods for specific projects 
●
Some projects are large strategic projects other are local ideas that are quick to implement  
●
Overview is key, the user needs to understand the aim, pro and cons, price and implementation 
resource requirements 
 
Quotes 
1.
Some smaller projects are started by and done in the local departments 
2.
Bigger projects or more strategic ones we will reach out to (external) partners figure out how can we 
solve this 
3.
We do a lot of projects with partners, either they contact us with an idea or we contact them 
4.
Our technical people are much more likely to use the tool(as described) as they look for solutions 
for technical problems 
5.
It depends who starts the projects, in some projects it may be a local idea that do not involve 
headquarters, some of very strategic that come from the board or management. 
6.
It would have to be easily understandable at a glance, so you can see what is the goal of this, what is 
the cost, with the resources involve, how much time do we need to use to estimate if it's correct 
project 
7.
When looking at projects or initiatives we try to figure out if it's the right match for us if there is 
any risk it legal or in branding issues and if everything looks good then we try to move it 
8.
We see potential in marketing but also to be able to see how good we are in this area. 
9.
We see the value both in marketing but also in risk management, or savings right now it’s not 
generally a pull from the customer side, but we do see more of them having their own CSR 
departments that need data on their products[value tage feature] 
10. We are right now working on a project on how we can reuse some of fabric especially polyester, 
here I try to reach out to my colleague he’s an engineer and he will try to research the field, and 
reach out to possible partners to collaborate with. 
11. The technical staff will go in and figure out what is possible with this area

Appendix 29.1
Brainstorming on features and insights
Brainstorm was used based on the Affinity chart to generate ideas 
for how we could present the tools and how they should be desig-
ned into a context.
It was a very loosely defined brainstorm that was looped some ti-
mes to enable the possibility to build upon the disucssion and the 
other’s ideas. It facilitated some good discussions and developed 
some interesting insights and ideas.
A brainstorm on each group from the Affinity Dia-
gram was performed resulting in 8 different A3 she-
ets with ideas on like the one on the right.

engineering background 
-

knowledge

textile company works with symbiosises and value chains

Appendix 34.1
Data merge development
Create data merge elements with Adobe InDesign  
As the number of tools will increase over time as more research is added to the Toolr database, a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
structured way of handling and extracting data as tool pages both for pdf/print and for digital
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
distribution was needed, this served to meet the requirement of easy maintenance and implement
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design changes as the users needs become better known.  
 
A data structure was created in spreadsheets where a number of functions were made to collect and
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
translate data into image links that could later be used to create a information and graphical rich
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
output. The data can be exported in a comma separated CVS file format readable by many systems
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
like Powerpoint, Word and Adobe InDesign, were templates were created for data merging.  
 
Graphical programs were used to create a number of graphics that corresponded to the data file.
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Templates were created with placeholder text and image fields and after linking the marge data it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
was possible to auto generate a custom page for each tool with 30 unique data elements and the
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
possibility to add over 50 more i future design changes requires it. 
 
Tool to generate data output  
The following guide describes how to auto-create and export merge data from the backstage 
framework and generate tool pages or other content based on tool data. 
 
●
In the backstage framework navigate to the ​Merge data​ sheet, ​copy the last row down 
to the next till it includes all the classified tools​, the function will automatically 
import data from the backstage framework sheet and the meta data sheet into the merge 
data sheet row. 
●
Go to ​File/Download as/​ and choses comma separated  .csv file, move the downloaded 
file to your ​/Toolr​  folder, make sure the file name is  ​toolr_mergedata.cvs​ and overwrite 
the existing ​toolr_mergedata.cvs. 
●
Create new data merged tool pages using ​/Toolr/Tool_page.indd​ in Adobe InDesign 
“​Data Merge​” feature. 
●
Open ​/Toolr/Tool_page.indd​ in Adobe InDesign, navigate to “​Data Merge​” and check 
Preview box.  
●
Edits on the Tool_page.indd will be reflected on all tool pages 
●
Press “​Create Merged Document​” to compile tool pages. Check the pages and if needed 
edit the page before exporting (edits here will only be reflected on the single pages) with the 
/File/Export…​ function.
