Appendix 34.2
Data merge development
Tool name Complex Value Optimisation for Resource Recovery (CVORR)
This approach sets out the key steps required for 
developing a dynamic, flexible, fully transparent 
valuation approach that goes beyond upstream and 
downstream processes involved in resource recovery 
from waste (RRfW) systems, making trade-offs explic-
it and eliminating partial and/or double-counting.
About this tool
Tool type Framework
Relevant for readiness level
Read more
Tool name Redesign for emissions reuse (R4ER)
The redesign for emissions reuse (R4ER) methodol-
ogy is a practical guidance on how manufacturing 
companies could address the challenges posed by 
the large amount of resources consumed during the 
operational stage of equipment’s life cycle involved 
in production processes.This research proposed 
methodology adopts IDEF0, LCA, MFCA and ARC 
tools in order to improve the environmental perfor-
mance of the production processes.
About this tool
Tool type Guideline
Relevant for readiness level
Read more
Tool name Conceptual design framework for polygeneration systems
A conceptual design framework consisting of scope 
definition, 3E (Energy, Environment and Economy) 
assessment and a comparison and a decision-making 
stage with the aim to facilitate the design of poly-
generation system followed by comparison of system 
performance among various design options.
About this tool
Tool type Framework
Relevant for readiness level
Read more
Tool name Sustainability rules for cascading use of bio-based polymers within a circular economy
Recommendations for designers and recyclers in 
order to promote DfR principles to enhance the 
cascading use of bio-based polymers.
About this tool
Tool type Guideline
Relevant for readiness level
Read more
Tool name Multiobjective optimization simulation framework
Decision support tool that uses global multiobjective 
optimization based on 1) the environmental impacts, 
evaluated within the framework of full life cycle 
assessment; and 2) process costs, evaluated by using 
rigorous process models.
About this tool
Tool type Simulation
Relevant for readiness level
Read more

Appendix 37.1
Questionaire for tool relevance
Testing the relevance of tools with company  
Dear S and D, 
 
Thank you very much for the last time. It was a great help in our specialty and really exciting to gain insight into your 
work with CSR. 
As agreed, based on our database, we found 8 'tools' that fit your readiness within the dimension 'Organization' that 
deals with the company's internal business capabilities to implement new concepts such as Circular Economics. 
We have chosen this dimension after the prioritization exercise we made for the workshop, where this was a priority 
priority both on relevance and inft time horizon. 
This assessment is based on the following: 
We have sent you 8 tools in A4 format (information pages.) We would like to ask you to skim these and assess whether 
or not you believe that the particular tool is relevant to the subject and whether the tool could have relevant to you in 
the company. 
 
Do not put yourself in the tool, but simply use the information given on the A4 page. 
 
Many thanks for the help! 
Sincerely. Nanna & Jeppe 
 
Tool # 1 - Remanufacturing Simulation Model (108)  
This tool is bothered by your readiness level within the "Business case" dimension as well as the "Processes & Tools" 
dimension. About these dimensions: 
 
Business Case / Business: To develop and present a clear business case is the key to selling the implementation of new 
concepts in the organization. 
 
Processes & Tools / Processes & Tools: Implementing new concepts and methods within the company involves a 
complete set of processes and tools to support conversion. 
Assess the relevance of this tool within the Business Case category. 
 
Assess the relevance of this tool within the "Processes & Tools" category. 
 
Assess the relevance of whether this tool could be relevant to business.

This tool is bugged based on your level of readiness within the "Business case"  
dimension. About this dimension:

implementation of new concepts in the organization.

REMANUFACTURING SIMULATION MODEL
Tool type
Simulation
Simulation framework for predicting the performance of 
remanufacturing systems operating under various production 
control policies within a digital environment, before the 
implementation in the real system. A generic and reconfigurable 
simulation model.
The article was authored by Gaspari L., Colucci L., Butzer S., Colledani M., Steinhilper R. 
Published in Journal of Remanufacturing Volume: 7 in 2017. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-84977260191&doi=10.1515%2fcer-2016-0013&partner-
ID=40&md5=27e3fb6680b418971e4c00aa49c4bd7b
Described in the 2017 article: 
Modularization in material flow simulation for managing production releases in reman-
ufacturing
Abstract from article
Remanufacturing is recognized as a major circular economy option to recover and upgrade func-
tions from post-use products. However, the inefficiencies associated with operations, mainly due 
to the uncertainty and variability of material flows and product conditions, undermine the growth 
of remanufacturing. With the objective of supporting the design and management of more pro-
ficient and robust remanufacturing processes, this paper proposes a generic and reconfigurable 
simulation model of remanufacturing systems. The developed model relies upon a modular 
framework that enables the user to handle multiple process settings and production control poli-
cies, among which token-based policies. Customizable to the characteristics of the process under 
analysis, this model can support logistics performance evaluation of different production control 
policies, thus enabling the selection of the optimal policy in specific business contexts. The pro-
posed model is applied to a real remanufacturing environment in order to validate and demon-
strate its applicability and benefits in the industrial settings. ¬© 2017, Springer Science+Business 
Media B.V.

A PRESERVATIVE DISASSEMBLY TRANSITION MATRIX
Tool type
Simulation
Guideline on how to incorporate automated and destructive 
operations while dismantling WEEE products. This involves the 
dismantling sequence, level, end-of-life options, adopted tools, 
labor and tool costs, profit, and regulation.
The article was authored by Cong L., Zhao F., Sutherland J.W. 
Published in Journal of Cleaner Production Volume: 149 in 2017. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-84937142047&doi=10.1016%2fj.jclepro.2015.06.124&part-
nerID=40&md5=a3b8ad9fe1e44c9e584c13e23f2f1eda
Described in the 2017 article: 
Integration of dismantling operations into a value recovery plan for circular economy
Abstract from article
Traditional linear production mode ‚Äútake-make-dispose‚Äù has brought about increasing re-
source scarcity, great environmental cost and endangered human health. To address the problem, 
the concept of circular economy has been introduced and accepted as a solution. In a circular 
economy, end-of-life (EOL) products can be introduced into multiple life cycles, which maximize 
the utility and value of components and materials. The economic viability of value recovery from 
an EOL product is greatly affected by the plan and technologies used for dismantling that prod-
uct. This paper presents an approach to find a profitable EOL strategy, which includes a meth-
od for generating a dismantling transition matrix and making decisions on the best dismantling 
sequence, level, and EOL options for components/parts. A preservative disassembly transition 
matrix is a description of possible ways to disassemble a product into individual parts without 
any damage. A guideline is created to include destructive and automated operations in order 
to expand a preservative disassembly matrix into a dismantling transition matrix, because dis-
mantling EOL products might produce more profit than pure preservative disassembly. Based on 
the dismantling transition matrix, binary integer programming is used to formulate EOL product 
value recovery while considering possible options for components/subassemblies as well as the 
transition cost between dismantling operations, tools costs, and recycling regulations or goals. A 
hard disk drive (HDD) is used as a case study to demonstrate the approach. ¬© 2017 Elsevier Ltd

Appendix 39.1
Interview guide for usability test
Test plan: 
1 hour usability test with stakeholders  
If the test person is unfamiliar with MATChE than 20 min, will be used to guide through the 
assessment. 
Participants: 
-
Interviewer 
-
Observator 
-
Informant  
10 minutes 
Introduction to us and MATChE + Pitch about Toolr  
10 minutes 
Interview about their work  
20 minutes 
MATChE Assessment  
30 minutes  
Test of Adobe XD website prototype  
10 minutes 
Evaluation questions + Thank you and goodbye 
 
Main research question: 
Is it usable?  
 
Test objectives are to gather insights and responses to the following topics:  
The usability  
-
content structure 
-
the language 
-
navigation 
-
functions  
The connection with MATChE 
The stand alone tool  
The value of tool information provided  
 
Introduction ​
(10 minutes) 
Initial questions ​
(10 minutes) 
We begin overall with a little around you so we can get an idea of ​who you are and what you do: 
-
Briefly describe who you are. What is your background for education, title, number of years in 
business or something else?  
-
Has circular economy and sustainability been on the agenda of your everyday life?  
-
Do you work with some specific methods / tools in your everyday life? Which ones?  
-
Can you put some words on the needs that you have in relation to working with tools / methods in 
your everyday life?  
-
Why could it be relevant to involve the MATChE platform in your work?  
 
==PAGE:251==
page 233
Appendix 39.2
Interview guide for usability test
 
Prototype testing​ (30 minutes)  
Introduction to testing  
-
We will test the solution, not you  
-
We will try to figure out how to improve the product.  
-
Test setup: 
-
You will perform the test on this computer in a prototype of a website made in Adobe XD 
-
It It is important to say that the prototype is at a very early stage and therefore there are 
features that are not possible to use. When we come to these, we will say so and we will ask 
you to explain what you would do in this situation to solve the task.  
-
You must try to describe your experience and explain your actions, so think high while 
performing the tasks and exploring the website.  
-
"If you forget to think high, I will remind you of it." 
-
I do not want to help or answer questions that have anything to do with the task.  
-
If you still have questions, feel free to ask and I will write them down and answer later. 
 
Our prototype consists of two parts previously described: 
-
Integrated part with my.MATChE platform 
-
Website Toolr.dk  
 
We start by saying that you are on my.MATChE.  
 
Tasks: 
1.
After your assessment on MATChE, you would like to read your results. Starting with "my 
readiness profile". Try to get an overview and tell your process.  
2.
Can you describe why this tool is selected for your profile?  
3.
You will find interest in one of the tools. Try to find more information about this. Tell how to 
evaluate whether the tool is relevant based on the given information. 
4.
From the page you are on now you would like to explore more tools within the X dimension 
relevant to your industry. How will you find these?  
5.
You would like to find an overview of the tools suggested for you. 
Can you find the most relevant tool 
6.
You want to understand the background of Toolr - who is behind the website and why are the tools 
selected? Try to find this information.  
 
Ask: ​Why did you do that? / What are you doing?

Appendix 39.3
Interview guide for usability test
Evaluation questions ​(10 minutes) 
-
How was your overall experience? 
-
What is your impression of the Toolr website?  
-
What do you think of design and layout?  
-
What do you think of the language?  
-
What benefit could the platform have?  
-
Could you imagine using the platform?  
-
Why / Why not?  
-
What would you use the platform for?  
-
What do you think about the amount of information about the tools?  
-
Did you lack any information to evaluate if they could be relevant to you?  
-
How did you experience the connection with MATChE 
-
Did you have any expectations for the platform after our introduction?  
-
If so, were they settled or not? ll.l ... .. 
-
(What could have helped you when some of the tasks were difficult?) 
-
What do you think it's on a website? 
-
Do you have any other comments?  
 
Note to us: 
-
Remember to note behavior 
-
If we are to demonstrate a task, we must not choose the same as the one they are going to get 
through  
-
the most valuable information available when users struggle to solve the task 
 
Outro  
We have now gone through the things we would like. We have received many good inputs that we 
can use in our further work on developing the page. 
Is there anything you think we need to talk about? 
We have noted your comments and they will be part of our further work towards developing and 
improving the website. 
Thanks for your time!

A TRANSFORMATIVE BUSINESS MODEL INNOVATION APPROACH
Tool type
Roadmap
A transformative business model innovation approach for 
shifting from 'doing things better' to 'doing better things'. 
Inspired by transition theory combining elements of systems 
thinking, value-inspired envisioning, reflexive design and co-
creation.
*The article was authored by Gorissen L., Vrancken K., Manshoven S. 
Published in Sustainability (Switzerland) Volume: 8 in 2016. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-84975267199&doi=10.1080%2f21681015.2016.1172124&
partnerID=40&md5=989b60b5a6a2389a372b4600aee95946
Described in the 2016 article: 
Transition thinking and business model innovation-towards a transformative business 
model and new role for the reuse centers of Limburg, Belgium
Abstract from article
The current dynamics of change, including climate change, resource depletion, energy transition, 
internet of things, etc. will have substantial impacts on the functioning of contemporary business 
models. Most firms and companies, however, still largely focus on efficiency strategies leading to 
sub-optimal solutions (reducing bad impact), rather than radically changing their business mod-
el to develop new transaction models more appropriate for today’s world (doing better things). 
However, persistent sustainability issues arising from our changing societal systems, require more 
radical and structural changes both within and between organizations to change the way we do 
business. There is limited empirically established research literature on how businesses can be 
more proactive in this regard and how they can move beyond “management of unsustainability”. 
In this paper, we present a transformative business model innovation approach inspired by transi-
tion theory combining elements of systems thinking, value-inspired envisioning, reflexive design 
and co-creation that was pioneered for a consortia of reuse centers in the province of Limburg, 
Belgium. We demonstrate that this approach contributed to the development of new business 
model concepts, was conducive for mobilizing support and resources to ensure follow up activity 
and holds potential to promote a more proactive role for businesses as agents of transformative 
change. ¬© 2016 by the authors.

STEP-BY STEP APPROACH TO THE EVALUATION PROCESS
Tool type
Guideline
Step-by-step stages for evaluating the environmental value 
propositions given by a specific CE business model.
*The article was authored by Manninen K., Koskela S., Antikainen R., Bocken N., Dahlbo H., Aminoff A. 
Published in Journal of Cleaner Production Volume: 171 in 2018. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-84999618293&partnerID=40&md5=d96aeff05169f9d-
1850d863a2f5770f9
Described in the 2018 article: 
Do circular economy business models capture intended environmental value proposi-
tions?
Abstract from article
The circular economy (CE) can be a driver for sustainability and CE can be promoted and support-
ed by the creation of new and innovative business models, which embed CE principles into their 
value propositions throughout the value chains. This study focuses on the environmental value 
propositions of the CE business models. The term environmental value proposition refers here to 
an absolute value being a promise of environmental improvement, which a company provides 
to the environment by its impacts throughout the whole value chain. The aim of this study is to 
outline a framework for evaluating the environmental value propositions of CE business models. 
The framework consists of an environmental value propositions table (EVPT) and a step-by-step 
approach towards an evaluation process. The framework was tested in three CE business model 
cases. The outlined framework enables a better understanding of circular economy principles, 
combining them with the environmental value proposition. With the framework, companies can 
plan and design new CE business models or they can verify intended environmental benefits and 
analyse their contribution to sustainability. The biggest challenges, when applying the frame-
work, were related to the estimation of environmental benefits gained from the environmental 
value propositions at the system level. In the future, intensive scientific work should concentrate 
on developing environmental assessment methods specifically for companies developing new CE 
business models. ¬© 2017 Elsevier Ltd

Tool id. 7
INDUSTRIAL SYMBIOSIS BUSINESS MODEL GUIDE
Tool type
Guideline
Identify different business models that firms can adopt to 
implement the Industrial symbiosis approach. For each model, 
highlighting how firms can create and get economic value from 
Industrial symbiosis.
*The article was authored by Fraccascia L., Magno M., Albino V. 
Published in Procedia Environmental Science, Engineering and Management Volume: 3 in 2016. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-84930448577&doi=10.1016%2fj.jclepro.2015.05.075&part-
nerID=40&md5=515567846ca9a23c0d111c3c01721dbb
Described in the 2016 article: 
Business models for industrial symbiosis: A guide for firms
Abstract from article
Industrial symbiosis (IS) is a collaborative approach concerning physical exchange of materials, 
energy, and services among different firms: accordingly, wastes produced by a given firm are ex-
ploited as inputs by other firms. This approach is able to generate economic and environmental 
benefits at the same time, the former for the involved firms and the latter for the collectivity as a 
whole. For these reasons, the implementation of IS is largely recommended. However, despite its 
huge potentialities, the IS approach seems to be actually underdeveloped and not fully exploited. 
Firms without any prior experience of IS exchanges suffer from lack of awareness about how to 
integrate the IS practice into their current business models and how to gain economic benefits 
from IS. Since the willingness to obtain economic benefits is the main driver pushing firms to 
implement the IS practice, this issue constitutes an important barrier to the development of new 
IS relationships. In this paper, we contribute to this issue by identifying the different business 
models that each firm can adopt to implement the IS approach. In particular, we identify several 
business models for both firms producing waste and firms requiring waste. For each model, we 
highlight how firms can create and get economic value from IS. Moreover, from the interaction 
among firms, each of them implementing its own business model, several business scenarios 
at inter-firm level can arise. These scenarios are also presented: for each of them, strengths and 
weaknesses are identified and a short case study is discussed. The identified models can be useful 
at the company level since they provide indications about how to integrate the IS approach within 
their current business model.

*The article was authored by Bocken N.M.P., de Pauw I., Bakker C., van der Grinten B. 
Published in Journal of Industrial and Production Engineering Volume: 33 in 2016. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-85041118689&doi=10.1016%2fj.jclepro.2017.12.168&part-
nerID=40&md5=2d73928586a1879069b833fc810ce783
Described in the 2016 article: 
Product design and business model strategies for a circular economy
Abstract from article
The transition within business from a linear to a circular economy brings with it a range of practical 
challenges for companies. The following question is addressed: What are the product design and 
business model strategies for companies that want to move to a circular economy model? This 
paper develops a framework of strategies to guide designers and business strategists in the move 
from a linear to a circular economy. Building on Stahel, the terminology of slowing, closing, and 
narrowing resource loops is introduced. A list of product design strategies, business model strat-
egies, and examples for key decision-makers in businesses is introduced, to facilitate the move to 
a circular economy. This framework also opens up a future research agenda for the circular econ-
omy. ¬© 2016 The Author(s). Published by Informa UK Limited, trading as Taylor & Francis Group.

AGENT-BASED SIMULATION APPROACH FOR BUSINESS MODEL AND CUSTOMER 
BEHAVIOR CHANGES
Tool type
Simulation
An agent-based simulation approach to study customer behavior 
in terms of their acceptance of new business models in Circular 
Economy (CE) context. Quantitative analysis tool for identifying 
proper marketing and pricing strategies. Testet on a business 
example of a washing machine.
*The article was authored by Lieder M., Asif F.M.A., Rashid A. 
Published in Autonomous Agents and Multi-Agent Systems Volume: 31 in 2017. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-84990923213&doi=10.3390%2fsu8090937&partner-
ID=40&md5=68ae807b5d264be0fc6c1edde0f3109f
Described in the 2017 article: 
Towards Circular Economy implementation: an agent-based simulation approach for 
business model changes
Abstract from article
This paper introduces an agent-based approach to study customer behavior in terms of their 
acceptance of new business models in Circular Economy (CE) context. In a CE customers are 
perceived as integral part of the business and therefore customer acceptance of new business 
models becomes crucial as it determines the successful implementation of CE. However, tools 
or methods are missing to capture customer behavior to assess how customers will react if an 
organization introduces a new business model such as leasing or functional sales. The purpose of 
this research is to bring forward a quantitative analysis tool for identifying proper marketing and 
pricing strategies to obtain best fit demand behavior for the chosen new business model. This 
tool will support decision makers in determining the impact of introducing new (circular) busi-
ness models. The model has been developed using an agent-based modeling approach which 
delivers results based on socio-demographic factors of a population and customers‚Äô relative 
preferences of product attributes price, environmental friendliness and service-orientation. The 
implementation of the model has been tested using the practical business example of a wash-
ing machine. This research presents the first agent-based tool that can assess customer behavior 
and determine whether introduction of new business models will be accepted or not and how 
customer acceptance can be influenced to accelerate CE implementation. The tool integrates 
socio-demographic factors, product utility functions, social network structures and inter-agent 
communication in order to comprehensively describe behavior on individual customer level. In 
addition to the tool itself the results of this research indicates the need for systematic marketing 
strategies which emphasize CE value propositions in order to accelerate customer acceptance 
and shorten the transition time from linear to circular. Agent-based models are emphasized as 
highly capable to fill the gap between diffusion-based penetration of information and resulting 
behavior in the form of purchase decisions. ¬© 2017, The Author(s).

*The article was authored by Witjes S., Lozano R. 
Published in Resources, Conservation and Recycling Volume: 112 in 2016. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-85033201629&doi=10.1007%2f978-3-319-45438-2_16&p
artnerID=40&md5=7bcc53de4faf2672c4a9703fb2a6f228
Described in the 2016 article: 
Towards a more Circular Economy: Proposing a framework linking sustainable public pro-
curement and sustainable business models
Abstract from article
Sustainability aims at addressing environmental and socio-economic issues in the long term. In 
general, the literature on sustainability has focused mainly on the environmental issues, whereas, 
more recently, a Circular Economy has been proposed as one of the latest concepts for address-
ing both the environmental and socio-economic issues. A Circular Economy aims at transforming 
waste into resources and on bridging production and consumption activities; however, there is 
still limited research focusing on these aspects. This paper addresses the link between procure-
ment and supply practices, and proposes changing from a traditional public procurement pro-
cess, based on product-selling business models, to a more service-oriented system. The paper 
proposes a framework to include technical and non-technical specifications of product/service 
combinations that improve resource usage efficiency through recovery. The framework also con-
siders socio-cultural specifications and physical and social proximity between the stakeholders 
in the procurement process. The framework is based on collaboration, which is a vital link be-
tween the public procurement process and the development of more sustainable business mod-
els, where the experience gained in the collaboration process serves as the bases for suppliers 
and procurers in improving their contribution to CE, whilst at the same time securing economic 
benefits for both parties. Although, in this process, the specification setting may take longer, the 
relationships between procurer and supplier tend to be longer lasting and stronger. This research 
shows that collaboration between procurers and suppliers throughout the procurement process 
can lead to reductions in raw material utilisation and waste generation, whilst promoting the 
development of new, more sustainable, business models. ¬© 2016 Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved.

Tool type
Simulation
A decision support tool that works at the intersection of multiple 
lifecycle design and business models in the circular economy 
context to identify effects on cost and CO2 emissions. Based on 
a multi-method simulation approach. Tested using a case with 
washing machines.
*The article was authored by Lieder M., Asif F.M.A., Rashid A., Miheliƒç A., Kotnik S. 
Published in International Journal of Advanced Manufacturing Technology Volume: 93 in 2017. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-85014902973&doi=10.1111%2fjiec.12554&partner-
ID=40&md5=432a2a764fc9a9293894b8c5c4b99786
Described in the 2017 article: 
Towards circular economy implementation in manufacturing systems using a multi-meth-
od simulation approach to link design and business strategy
Abstract from article
The recent circular economy movement has raised awareness and interest about untapped envi-
ronmental and economic potential in the manufacturing industry. One of the crucial aspects in 
the implementation of circular or closed-loop manufacturing approach is the design of circular 
products. While it is obvious that three post-use strategies, i.e., reuse, remanufacturing, and recy-
cling, are highly relevant to achieve loop closure, it is enormously challenging to choose ‚Äúthe 
right‚Äù strategy (if at all) during the early design stage and especially at the single component 
level. One reason is that economic and environmental impacts of adapting these strategies are 
not explicit as they vary depending on the chosen business model and associated supply chains. 
In this scenario, decision support is essential to motivate adaptation of regenerative design strat-
egies. The main purpose of this paper is to provide reliable decision support at the intersection of 
multiple lifecycle design and business models in the circular economy context to identify effects 
on cost and CO2 emissions. The development of this work consists of a systematic method to 
quantify design effort for different circular design options through a multi-method simulation 
approach. The simulation model combines an agent-based product architecture and a discrete 
event closed-loop supply chain model. Feasibility of the model is tested using a case of a washing 
machine provided by Gorenje d.d. Firstly, design efforts for reuse, remanufacturing, and recycling 
are quantified. Secondly, cost and emissions of different design options are explored with differ-
ent business model configurations. Finally, an optimization experiment is run to identify the most 
cost-effective combination of reused, remanufactured, and recycled components for a business 
model chosen on the basis of the explorative study results. ¬© 2017, The Author(s).

Tool id. 16
CIRCULAR DESIGN FRAMEWORK
Tool type
Framework
Circular design strategies mapped out against the circular 
business model archetypes and their value creation. A taxonomy 
of DfX design strategies for Circular economy.
*The article was authored by Moreno M., De los Rios C., Rowe Z., Charnley F. 
Published in Sustainability (Switzerland) Volume: 8 in 2016. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-85028965819&doi=10.1007%2f978-3-319-45438-
2_3&partnerID=40&md5=a682f1462d03b9092a61c4598756d664
Described in the 2016 article: 
A conceptual framework for circular design
Abstract from article
Design has been recognised in the literature as a catalyst to move away from the traditional model 
of take-make-dispose to achieve a more restorative, regenerative and circular economy. As such, 
for a circular economy to thrive, products need to be designed for closed loops, as well as be 
adapted to generate revenues. This should not only be at the point of purchase, but also during 
use, and be supported by low-cost return chains and reprocessing structures, as well as effective 
policy and regulation. To date, most academic and grey literature on the circular economy has 
focused primarily on the development of new business models, with some of the latter studies 
addressing design strategies for a circular economy, specifically in the area of resource cycles and 
design for product life extension. However, these studies primarily consider a limited spectrum of 
the technical and biological cycles where materials are recovered and restored and nutrients (e.g., 
materials, energy, water) are regenerated. This provides little guidance or clarity for designers 
wishing to design for new circular business models in practice. As such, this paper aims to address 
this gap by systematically analysing previous literature on Design for Sustainability (DfX) (e.g., 
design for resource conservation, design for slowing resource loops and whole systems design) 
and links these approaches to the current literature on circular business models. A conceptual 
framework is developed for circular economy design strategies. From this conceptual framework, 
recommendations are made to enable designers to fully consider the holistic implications for de-
sign within a circular economy. ¬© 2016 by the authors.

Tool id. 17
BECE (BACKCASTING AND ECO-DESIGN FOR THE CIRCULAR ECONOMY)
Tool type
Framework
Coupled together, backcasting and eco-design can be used as 
powerful symbiotic tools, with the former helping to set long-
term targets and identify practical steps to achieving them and 
the latter enabling realization of the targets for product and 
service performance.
*The article was authored by Mendoza J.M.F., Sharmina M., Gallego-Schmid A., Heyes G., Azapagic A. 
Published in Journal of Industrial Ecology Volume: 21 in 2017. 
Found via Scopus: https://www.scopus.com/inward/record.uri?eid=2-s2.0-85018489782&doi=10.1186%2fs13243-016-0025-z&part-
nerID=40&md5=d93347651589da17f3dc57dbac6931de
Described in the 2017 article: 
Integrating Backcasting and Eco-Design for the Circular Economy: The BECE Framework
Abstract from article
The circular economy (CE) is essential for decoupling economic growth from resource consump-
tion and environmental impacts. However, effective implementation requires a systemic change 
across supply chains, involving both technological and nontechnological innovations. Frame-
works are beginning to emerge to foster CE thinking in organizations. However, literature review 
carried out as part of this research has revealed gaps in their ability to fulfil CE requirements. 
Furthermore, few frameworks provide support on how CE requirements may be implemented. To 
address these issues, this article presents a new framework, BECE (backcasting and eco-design for 
the circular economy), to ensure that businesses can implement CE requirements more readily. 
BECE empowers organizations to tackle the CE holistically by embedding the concept into cor-
porate decision making and by bringing operational and systems thinking together, thus increas-
ing the likelihood of successful implementation. The potential of the BECE framework was tested 
through a pilot workshop focusing on the development of a CE business model through redesign 
of products and supply chains. Using vacuum cleaners as an illustrative case study, several prod-
uct design and supply-chain alternatives were identified, including the development of scenarios 
and action plans for their implementation at the business level. Although the case study focuses 
on a particular product, the BECE framework is generic and applicable across different products 
and business sectors. ¬© 2017 by Yale University
